Received 
      24th January 2021
    , Accepted 30th March 2021First published on 30th March 2021AbstractNoble metals have received widespread applications in the field of catalysis due to their unique intrinsic properties and irreplaceable catalytic activities. However, in consideration of the scarcity and high cost, maximizing the efficiency of noble metals for catalysis is of prime importance in the societal pursuit of sustainable energy. In recent years, noble-metal single-atom catalysts (NMSACs) with well-defined structures have gained great research attention due to their maximum atom utilization efficiency (100%), distinct active sites and high catalytic activity and selectivity. This review comprehensively discusses the recent advancements in NMSACs for catalytic applications. Firstly, various fabrication strategies for NMSACs are introduced with a focus on how to effectively stabilize single noble-metal atoms on appropriate substrates and prevent their migration and aggregation. Subsequently, some advanced characterization techniques are presented to precisely probe the noble-metal active sites at the atomic level, which is critical to investigate the structure of NMSACs. Furthermore, we provide a comprehensive summary of various types of NMSACs for the applications of thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis, and photocatalysis, with special emphasis on the structure–activity relationships and the underlying catalytic mechanisms. Finally, the remaining challenges and future opportunities are provided for guiding the rational design of advanced NMSACs toward various catalytic processes in the chemical transformation and energy conversion fields.
Yinlong Zhu
Yinlong Zhu received his PhD degree from College of Chemical Engineering, Nanjing Tech University, in 2017. Now he works as a Discovery Early Career Researcher Award (DECRA) Fellow at Monash University, Australia. His current research focuses on the development of functional materials (especially perovskite oxides) as ORR/OER/HER/NRR electrocatalysis for the application of electrochemical energy technologies, such as solid oxide fuel cells, metal–air batteries and water splitting. He has published over 30 papers as the first author/corresponding author in Nat. Commun., Adv. Mater., Angew. Chem., Int. Ed., Energy Environ. Sci., Matter, Nano Lett., Adv. Energy Mater., Adv. Funct. Mater., etc.
Huanting Wang
Huanting Wang is a Professor and ARC Australian Laureate Fellow in the Department of Chemical Engineering, and the director of the Monash Centre for Membrane Innovation at Monash University, Australia. His current research focuses on the development of nanostructured materials and membranes for gas separation, water treatment, chiral separation, ion transport and separation, and electrochemical energy applications. He has co-authored over 300 papers in these areas.

Broader context
Clean energy and environmental technologies are essential for pursuing a sustainable future. To boost the rate and selectivity of key chemical reactions involved in these technologies, high-performance catalysts are necessary. Noble-metal nanoparticles have always been regarded as benchmark catalysts with reasonably high activity, but the scarcity and high cost limit their large-scale practical applications. Reducing the dosage and simultaneously maximizing the catalytic efficiency is highly desirable for making full use of noble metals. Noble-metal single-atom catalysts (NMSACs), with well-defined structures and maximum atom utilization efficiency, are emerging alternatives to noble-metal nanoparticles due to better catalytic performance. In this review, we present a comprehensive overview of recent advances of NMSACs in various catalytic applications including thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis, and photocatalysis. Synthetic strategies, characterization techniques and structure–activity correlation of NMSACs are highlighted. We also conclude existing challenges and provide an outlook for future development of NMSACs. This review is expected to provide readers with a comprehensive understanding of NMSACs for catalysis and offer some guidelines for the design of advanced catalysts in the future.

1. Introduction
With the growing global concerns about the energy crisis and environment pollution caused by the depletion of non-renewable fossil fuels, tremendous efforts have been made to explore high-efficiency technologies for chemical transformations and energy conversion, while most of them depend heavily on various catalytic processes (e.g., thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis and photocatalysis).1–5 Therefore, designing efficient catalysts with high selectivity and a fast catalytic reaction rate is of paramount significance and has been a longstanding goal in modern society.6,7 Noble-metal-based catalysts are widely regarded as state-of-the-art catalysts due to their unique intrinsic properties and irreplaceable catalytic activities.8,9 For example, noble metals including platinum (Pt), ruthenium (Ru) and gold (Au) supported on carbon substrates display outstanding CO oxidation performance. Pt-Based materials are the benchmark electrocatalysts for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR), hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) and methanol oxidation reaction (MOR). Palladium (Pd)-based materials with high hydrogenation activity are widely used for hydrogenation of acetylene. However, due to the scarcity of noble metals on the earth (less than 3 wt% among all elements) and costliness, how to make the best of these noble resources remains a big issue for large-scale industrial applications.10–13 Therefore, reducing the amounts of noble metals used and meanwhile maximizing the utilization efficiency of noble metals for catalysis are highly desirable in consideration of the sustainable and economic aspects.14–16
Single-atom catalysts (SACs), with isolated single metal atoms dispersed on a specific solid support, have recently emerged as a new research frontier in materials science and catalysis.17,18 The concept of SACs was first proposed by Zhang et al. in 2011.19 In their pioneering study, they prepared an SAC with isolated single Pt atoms supported on iron oxide (Pt1/FeOx), which shows excellent catalytic activity for CO oxidation. Since then, SACs have been widely investigated and developed rapidly in the field of catalysis. As compared with heterogeneous catalysts, SACs possess homogenized active centers for catalytic reactions (similar to homogeneous catalysts), consequently keeping the combined merits of heterogeneous catalysts with stable properties, high tolerance to harsh conditions and excellent recyclability, and homogeneous catalysts with superior activity and selectivity.20,21 SACs have received extensive research attention mainly because of the following characteristics: (i) atomically dispersed metal centers with 100% active atom utilization; (ii) low atomic coordination number of the active center; (iii) quantum size effects with electron confinement; and (iv) metal–support interactions.18,22–24 These interesting properties of SACs can promote the enhancement of both the reaction rate and selectivity for various catalytic processes, including thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis, and photocatalysis.4,25 Inspired by the above considerations, SACs provide a great opportunity in minimizing the usage of noble metals with 100% atom-utilization efficiency and performing the perfect approach for constructing cost-effective noble metal catalysts. Recently, a variety of noble-metal SACs (NMSACs) featuring high selectivity, catalytic activity and stability, superior to their nanocluster and nanoparticle (NP) counterparts, have been reported in diverse catalytic reactions.21,26,27 To date, several reviews relevant to SACs have been published, but, to the best of our knowledge, very few paid special attention to the catalytic applications of NMSACs. Accordingly, a comprehensive review on such a topic is timely and important.
In this article, we present an overview of the recent advancements in NMSACs for catalytic applications over the past few years (Fig. 1). This review is structured in the following parts. Firstly, various synthetic strategies for the preparation of NMSACs are summarized and the significant role of the formation of strong chemical interactions between atomic metal sites and appropriate supports is discussed. Then, we introduce the characterization techniques for probing and identifying the noble-metal active sites at the atomic level, which is vital for further investigation of SACs. In addition, the promising potential for the application of NMSACs in various catalytic systems is reviewed in detail. The catalytic applications are classified into 3 categories: thermocatalysis (e.g., CO oxidation, water–gas shift reactions, and selective hydrogenation and dehydrogenation reactions), electrocatalysis (e.g., the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR), hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), oxygen evolution reaction (OER), CO2 reduction reaction (CO2RR) and N2 reduction reaction (N2RR)), and photocatalysis (e.g., photocatalytic H2 evolution and CO2 reduction). In the discussion, how the unique electronic and structural characteristics of NMSACs boost their catalytic performance is mainly focused on. Finally, we provide the current research challenges and an outlook for the future development of NMSACs.
 Fig. 1  Schematic illustration of the synthetic strategies, characterization techniques and catalytic applications of NMSACs summarized in this review. 
2. Synthetic strategies of NMSACs
Developing facile and effective strategies is a prerequisite for fabricating stable NMSACs. Due to the high surface energy of isolated atoms, it remains a challenge to successfully disperse metal atoms onto a suitable support surface.28 In order to investigate the catalytic performance of atomically dispersed noble metal catalysts, a variety of strategies have been reported in the past decade, such as the wet chemistry strategy, physical and chemical deposition and high-temperature pyrolysis. Based on recent advances, we will discuss several different approaches for the synthesis of NMSACs.
2.1. Wet chemistry strategy
The wet chemistry strategy is commonly employed to synthesize NMSACs, because it can not only be operated easily without specialized equipment, but also promote large-scale production.29 In the synthetic approach, the main objective is to successfully anchor isolated metal species that are usually contained in the precursor material onto the support and prevent agglomeration during the process. Experimentally, strong metal–support interactions between the single noble metal atoms and the support can be achieved by effectively coordinating the ligands in the metal complexes with the surface groups on the substrate. This widely used strategy usually consists of three sequential processes. First, the metal precursor is introduced and loaded on the support by deposition–precipitation, wet impregnation/ion-exchange, or coprecipitation. Then, the precursor material is dried to remove the solvent followed by heat treatment. Lastly, reduction or activation procedures are required to fabricate NMSACs.30 In practice, due to the agglomeration tendency of single atoms at a high metal loading and limited anchoring sites on the solid substrate, a metal precursor with a low concentration is generally preferred to be used for this method. However, the resulting low metal loadings (usually below 1 wt%) may hinder the catalytic performance. Therefore, enhancing the metal–support interaction in the synthesis is highly desirable for increasing the accessible metal active sites on the support.26,31 In this section, four fundamental approaches related to the wet chemistry strategy including the impregnation method, deposition–precipitation method, co-precipitation method and spatial confinement strategy are summarized in detail.

Impregnation method. 
The impregnation method including incipient wetness impregnation and dry impregnation is an economical and simple strategy to prepare NMSACs. However, it suffers from difficulty in achieving uniformly distributed atomic metal sites on the support. In this technique, several key parameters should be considered to control the metal dispersion, such as the support type and the functional groups on the surface, the concentration and type of the metal precursor complexes, and the pH of the solution.30,32 For instance, Wang and co-workers employed mesoporous sulfur-doped carbon (denoted as meso_S–C) as the support to stabilize a series of noble metal single atoms such as Pt, Pd, Ir and Ru by the incipient wetness impregnation method, followed by thermal reduction at 250 °C in a 5 vol% H2/Ar atmosphere (Fig. 2a). The final metal loading on the support was up to 10 wt%. This study confirms that the enhanced formic acid oxidation reaction (FAOR) and quinoline hydrogenation activity can be attributed to the strong chemical interactions between sulfur and atomic metal sites.33 Recently, Bulushev and co-workers also prepared a series of atomically dispersed Pt-group metals supported on N-doped carbon nanofibers via the impregnation method. Density functional theory (DFT) modeling indicates that the single metal atoms can be effectively stabilized by a pair of pyridinic N atoms, which are situated at the open edge of the carbon sheet. Such strong metal–support interactions (chelate binding) can lead to superior stability and enhanced H2 selectivity from formic acid decomposition.34
 Fig. 2  (a) Synthesis of metal/meso_S–C (metal = Ru, Rh, Pd, Ir, and Pt) via a wet chemistry strategy. Reproduced with permission.33 Copyright 2019, American Association for the Advancement of Science. (b) Synthesis of a single-atom Pd1/graphene catalyst via an ALD strategy. Reproduced with permission.50 Copyright 2015, American Chemical Society. (c) Synthesis of atomically dispersed Pt deposited on various solid substrates (multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs), mesoporous carbon (MC), ZnO nanowires, graphene and TiO2 nanoparticles) via an ice-photochemical strategy. Reproduced with permission.53 Copyright 2017, Nature Publishing Group. (d) Synthesis of SA-Rh/CN via high-temperature pyrolysis. Reproduced with permission.63 Copyright 2020, Nature Publishing Group. 


Deposition–precipitation method. 
The deposition–precipitation method is one of the effective and facile strategies to synthesize highly active nano-catalysts. In the synthesis, the metal precursors are added into a suspension of the target support (usually metal oxide particles) to form a uniformly mixed suspension. Then, under stirring conditions, metal ion complexes can be deposited on the support surface by controlling the pH of the suspension. This approach is useful for fabricating uniformly distributed noble metal single atoms.30,35 For example, Zhang and co-workers reported isolated Au3+ ions loaded on a zirconia (ZrO2) support fabricated by the deposition–precipitation method, followed by a calcination process. The isolated Au3+ ions on the surface are discovered to be the active sites for 1,3-butadiene hydrogenation.36 However, deposition–precipitation may not be suitable for producing catalysts with a high level of metal loading, because small metal (hydroxide) clusters/NPs could be generated in the solution before the adsorption of metal ion complexes onto the support. Moreover, the nucleation process is more likely to occur in the solution instead of on the support, which could be another concern for fabricating NMSACs.30,37


Co-Precipitation method. 
The co-precipitation method is an approach involving the simultaneous precipitation of two or more metal cations by adding precipitants in the solution. Different active species are uniformly distributed in the obtained solid products. Although a critical issue for this method to prepare SACs is that the single metal atoms could be buried in the support crystallites or at the interface of the support agglomerates, this strategy is still commonly applied to fabricate NMSACs as a result of the simple procedures, short synthetic period and easy to control experimental conditions.19,30,38 Recently, a report by Qiao and co-workers showed that single Pt atoms (0.17 wt% Pt loading) can be stabilized by iron oxide (FeOx) surface defects. During the process, both the pH of the solution and the co-precipitation temperature were finely controlled. It is noteworthy that the charge-transfer mechanism from individual Pt atoms to the FeOx surface has been confirmed to be effective for stabilizing Pt atoms on the support.19 Likewise, ultrastable atomically dispersed Au anchored on FeOx (Au1/FeOx) was also successfully prepared for CO oxidation by the same method.39


Spatial confinement strategy. 
NMSACs can be achieved by spatially trapping single noble metal atoms into cages at a molecular scale through an efficient spatial confinement strategy. This strategy can effectively stabilize the single atoms and avoid migration during the process. Typically, two steps are involved in this approach. The first step is to separate and encapsulate mononuclear metal precursors with the assistance of porous materials, such as covalent-organic frameworks (COFs), metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) and zeolites, which have uniform pore structures. Then, atomically dispersed metal species stabilized on the support could be obtained by removing the precursors.31,40 The spatial confinement strategy has been employed to fabricate NMSACs in several recent studies. For instance, Kistler et al. reported a single-site platinum catalyst fabricated by spatially confining a [Pt(NH3)4]2+ complex into the pores of zeolite KLTL. During the process, the metal complexes can occupy the location of Na+ and K+via ion exchange and are bonded to oxygen atoms on the zeolite surface. Then, after oxidation at 633 K, single Pt atoms stabilized in the KLTL zeolite can be achieved. This study indicates that a crystalline support with uniform surface sites is beneficial for fabricating single-site catalysts.41 In addition to zeolites, MOFs and COFs have also been developed to prepare NMSACs. For example, atomically dispersed Ru on N-doped carbon were produced by the spatial confinement strategy using the UiO-66-NH2 MOF as the carbon support. Interestingly, the uncoordinated amine groups (–NH2) in the skeleton of UiO-66-NH2 play a key role in stabilizing RuCl3. The strong coordination between Ru ions and –NH2 can effectively prevent Ru3+ migration. After high temperature pyrolysis and HF treatment, single Ru atoms anchored on N-doped porous carbon were obtained. It indicates the great potential of MOFs as a desirable support for the fabrication of NMSACs.42

2.2. Physical and chemical deposition
In addition to the wet chemistry strategy, the synthesis of NMSACs can also be achieved by physical and chemical deposition.

Physical deposition. 
Physical vapor deposition (PVD) is a deposition process involving the vaporization of metal precursors such as metals and metal alloys using a physical method (e.g., bombardment and heating). This technique has been widely applied in electronics, materials, aerospace and other fields due to the advantages of the simple process, environmental friendliness, strong binding force with the substrate and uniform film formation.43,44 Recent studies have employed this method for the preparation of atomically dispersed noble metal catalysts. For example, Sykes's group synthesized Pt–Cu single-atom alloys (SAAs) for the dehydrogenation of formic acid by depositing 0.01 ML of Pt on a Cu(111) crystal surface via PVD. From the scanning tunneling microscope (STM) image, the observable individual Pt sites on the Cu(111) surface confirm that Pt has been successfully alloyed with Cu(111).45 Using the same method, single-site Pt atoms dispersed on a well-defined copper oxide (Cu2O) film were fabricated by Therrien and co-workers in 2018 for CO oxidation.46


Chemical deposition. 
The atomic layer deposition (ALD) technique, a vapor-phase deposition process, is an effective approach for uniformly depositing metal catalytic materials on a selected large surface area substrate in a self-limiting and layer-by-layer manner. In the synthesis, the solid substrate surface is repeatedly exposed to gaseous precursors.47–49 This strategy offers a great opportunity to investigate the structure of catalysts and the relative catalytic processes, because the composition and thickness of the films can be precisely controlled at an atomic level. Generally, soft materials (e.g., graphene) and metal oxides are applied to load metal precursors in the ALD strategy.40,50 For instance, in 2015, Lu's group obtained atomically dispersed Pd on graphene by precisely controlling the oxygen functional groups on the support for anchoring palladium hexafluoroacetylacetate (Pd(hfac)2) (Fig. 2b).50 Both microscope and synchrotron characterization verified the existence of single Pd atoms on graphene, further confirming successful immobilization of isolated Pd atoms on anchoring sites after the precursor–support reaction and ligand removal under the ALD conditions.50 The ALD strategy was also applied for the preparation of isolated Pd atoms on the surface of graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) in Huang's study.51 g-C3N4 is a suitable support material owing to the excellent thermal stability and effective six-fold cavities for anchoring single Pd atoms. During the process, single-atom Pd/C3N4 was successfully obtained under one Pd ALD cycle, However, when the ALD cycles were increased to ten, Pd nanoparticles were formed. Although the PVD and ALD technique are effective for the fabrication of NMSACs through vapor transportation, their mass production is still limited by the harsh conditions such as a high vacuum environment and expensive equipment, and difficulty in precisely controlling the anchoring sites on the solid substrate.40,52

2.3. Photochemical strategy
In the past decades, the photochemical strategy has been considered as one of the effective routes for the fabrication of metal nanocrystals. During the process, controlling the nucleation and growth of solids is of vital importance for adjusting the particle size and morphology of nanomaterials.40,53,54 To prepare atomically dispersed noble metal catalysts, such a simple and time-saving method can effectively prevent the agglomeration of noble metals in comparison to the traditional wet chemistry strategy. Moreover, the process is more scalable than the ALD technique. Typically, extra chemical or physical treatment is not required for the generated catalysts after photochemical reduction.55
Recently, Liu and co-workers fabricated single Pd atoms (loading up to 0.5 at%) on an ultrathin titanium oxide (TiO2) support functionalized with ethylene glycolate (EG) under ultraviolet (UV)-light treatment. Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) verified the successful formation of single Pd atoms on the TiO2 support without any observation of Pd nanoparticles. In this study, the plentiful Pd–O bonds formed with the assistance of UV-generated EG radicals have been proved to be effective in removing Cl− and stabilizing isolated Pd atoms on the support.56 In addition to the conventional photochemical strategy, Wu's team reported that atomically dispersed Pt was successfully deposited on various solid substrates, including multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs), mesoporous carbon (MC), ZnO nanowires, graphene and TiO2 nanoparticles using an ice-photochemical strategy (Fig. 2c). Among these catalysts, Pt SACs stabilized on the MC material exhibit superior HER performance compared to commercial Pt/C. In the synthesis, the formation of an ice layer by rapidly freezing H2PtCl6 aqueous solution with liquid nitrogen is the key step for effectively confining isolated Pt ions and limiting their aggregation. Afterwards, the ice layer is irradiated with UV light at an extremely low temperature under dark conditions. Lastly, different Pt SACs are achieved by mixing the frozen solution with various support dispersions. It is noteworthy that the Pt SAs can be obtained in the solution before mixing with the dispersion of supports. The ice-photochemical strategy offers a promising route for the green fabrication of SACs.53
2.4. High-temperature pyrolysis
High-temperature pyrolysis is recognized as one of the facile and powerful methods for the synthesis of SACs on various supports. As is well known, this strategy involves thermal decomposition of suitable precursors at high temperatures in a controlled inert atmosphere (e.g., NH3, Ar, H2 and N2). Several different sources of supports have been applied in the synthesis, such as MOFs, small molecule precursors and polymers.57,58 Among these materials, MOFs have attracted extensive attention in recent years due to the unique porous structure and diverse compositions. The obtained SACs usually demonstrate superior conductivity and catalytic performance. However, the main obstacle for this strategy is the aggregation tendency of metal atoms at high temperatures. Generally, in this case, the excess metal particles formed in the synthesis can be effectively removed by acid treatment, leaving only the desired isolated metal sites on the support.28,59,60 Moreover, MOFs can be adopted as suitable cages to confine metal precursors. For example, Xiao et al. prepared a catalyst with Ir single atoms on N-doped carbon for a high-performance ORR using a confined pyrolysis method.61 In this study, an Ir-containing derivative of zeolitic imidazolate framework-8 (ZIF-8) was selected as the precursor. Ir acetylacetonate (Ir(acac)3) with a molecular diameter of 9.8 Å can be in situ encapsulated by ZIF-8 with a cavity diameter of 11.6 Å. After high temperature pyrolysis (950 °C) under an Ar/H2 (10% H2) atmosphere, individual Ir atoms uniformly distributed in N-doped carbon were achieved (Ir mass loading: 0.2 wt%). The successful synthesis of Ir-SAC can be ascribed to the space-confinement effect and generation of nitrogen anchoring sites after Zn evaporation at elevated temperatures.61 Ru-SAC and Rh-SAC have also been successfully prepared by a similar strategy (Fig. 2d).62,63 In another study, Bai and co-workers reported an atomically dispersed Ru catalyst prepared by annealing a mixture precursor of ruthenium chloride (RuCl3) and graphene oxide (GO). The final Ru loading was 0.76 wt%. Both the high surface area of GO and low Ru content ensure a high dispersion of Ru atoms.64
2.5. Galvanic replacement
The galvanic replacement strategy has been applied in recent studies to fabricate a variety of nanomaterials with controllable morphology and composition. Typically, a redox reaction incorporating reduction of the target metal ions in the solution and oxidation of a sacrificial template (generally a metal and metal oxide) is involved in this approach. The electrical reduction potential between the metal ions and the selected template drives the reaction to take place.65–67 When they are in contact with the solution, the metal ions can be effectively reduced and deposited over the substrate surface. Very recently, the galvanic replacement approach has become highly desirable for preparing NMSACs, due to the advantages of the short reaction time and the possibility to obtain high-efficiency and low-cost products with large pore volumes.65,68
In 2016, a report by Wang and co-workers demonstrated that single Rh atoms can be stabilized on a cobalt oxide (CoO) surface via the galvanic replacement strategy. In the synthesis, Na3RhCl6 solution was injected into a CoO nanosheet-containing solution firstly, and then a redox reaction between Rh atoms and CoO nanosheets took place during the mixing process. Co atoms on the substrate surface can be substituted by the Rh atoms in the solution. After the galvanic replacement reaction, single Rh atoms can be clearly observed on the support surface by the STEM technique.69 Similarly, atomically dispersed Pd catalysts stabilized on various metal oxide supports such as ZnO, TiO2 and Cu2O were successfully prepared and investigated by Zheng's group. Among these catalysts, single Pd atoms on Cu2O exhibit the highest catalytic activity and selectivity of alkenes formed from the semihydrogenation of alkynes. During the process, Pd atoms from the Pd(CH3CN)2Cl2 precursor were deposited on the Cu2O support via galvanic displacement in the solution (Pd2+ + Cu1+ → Pd1+ + Cu2+). The as-formed distinct structure (Pd–(O–Cu)2) and oxidation state of Pd (+1) led to the excellent performance, further confirming the critical role of the support effect in the process.70 In addition to single metal atoms anchored directly on supports, galvanic replacement is also an effective strategy for the fabrication of SAAs. For instance, Pt partially replaced Ni atoms on several different Ni-containing supports (e.g., activated carbon (AC) and carbon nanotubes (CNTs)) to form SAAs, which were composed of single Pt atoms (160 ppm) supported on Ni particles. The superior activity can be attributed to the unique electronic properties of the Ni-based catalysts modified by single Pt atoms.71 Such a facile and effective strategy opens the possibility for obtaining controlled supported SACs.
2.6. Other strategies
Apart from the above-mentioned strategies for the synthesis of noble metal atomic-scale catalysts, several other effective approaches have also been reported in recent years. For example, a microwave-assisted strategy was applied to stabilize noble metals including Pt, Ir, Ag, and Pd on a mesoporous g-C3N4 support by Vorobyeva and co-workers. Owing to the high yield and short reaction time, microwave heating is a promising approach to prepare uniformly distributed SACs with a high yield.72 Similarly, atomically dispersed Pt on aniline-stacked graphene (AG) was also prepared by the reduction of PtCl62− ions to single Pt atoms on the substrate surface via microwave irradiation (Fig. 3a).73 In addition, a thermal emitting strategy has been developed for the fabrication of SACs recently. For instance, Qu and co-workers synthesized single Pt atoms on defective graphene (DG) via this simple route for catalyzing the oxidation of various organosilanes and the HER (Fig. 3b). In the synthesis, volatile Pt(NH3)x species were formed via the coordination between Pt atoms from the bulk Pt net and ammonia from decomposition of dicyandiamide firstly, and then trapped by the DG support.74 The thermal emitting method offers guidance for the facile fabrication of SACs using bulk metals as precursors. Besides, the electrochemical deposition method is also a promising strategy to obtain NMSACs due to the controllable size and amount of the deposited metal. Taking single Pt atoms supported on MoS2 as an example, a three-electrode system was constructed using MoS2/graphite paper (GP) as the counter electrode (CE) and Pt foil as the working electrode (WE) (Fig. 3c). During the process, Pt atoms from the WE were easily deposited on the MoS2 support through cathodic growth in an electrochemical solution.26,52 According to recently published reports, various strategies such as the surfactant-assisted strategy (Fig. 3d),75 ball-milling method (Fig. 3e)76 and amine-induced-reduction method (Fig. 3f)77 have also been confirmed to be effective for the synthesis of NMSACs.
 Fig. 3  (a) Synthesis of Pt SAS/AG via microwave irradiation. Reproduced with permission.73 Copyright 2019, The Royal Society of Chemistry. (b) Synthesis of Pt SA/DG via the thermal emitting strategy. Reproduced with permission.74 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. (c) Synthesis of single-atom Pt deposited on an MoS2/GP electrode via electrochemical deposition. Reproduced with permission.52 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. (d) Synthesis of PtSA–MNS via the surfactant-assisted strategy. Reproduced with permission.75 Copyright 2019, Wiley-VCH. (e) Synthesis of Pd1/ZnO via the ball-milling method.76 Reproduced with permission.76 Copyright 2019, Elsevier. (f) Synthesis of Au1Nx single-site/C3N4via the amine-induced-reduction method. Reproduced with permission.77 Copyright 2018, Elsevier. 
2.7. Summary
In summary, the development of highly active and robust NMSACs can be achieved through various synthetic strategies in consideration of some key factors, such as the time, cost, procedural complexity and yield. To compare these strategies more clearly, their respective advantages and disadvantages have been summarized in Table 1. Generally, the synthetic approaches can be divided into two categories, “bottom-up” (direct-synthesis) and “top-down” (post-synthesis) routes, based on the different starting precursor materials.78,79 For the former, the wet chemistry strategy is considered as one of the most promising and effective “bottom-up” strategies to prepare NMSACs due to the simple procedure and low cost. However, the low metal loadings and the difficulty in controlling the location of the active sites on the support may hamper their catalytic performance and practical industrial applications.79,80 In addition, although PVD is a simple and environmentally friendly approach, and ALD can accurately control the loading amount of NMSACs and provide the opportunity to explore the structure–performance relationships, they suffer from some problems including the cost and harsh conditions (e.g., expensive equipment and a high vacuum environment) and the difficulty in controlling the anchoring sites on the solid substrate.80,81 As for galvanic replacement, this low-cost and energy-efficient method can not only produce low-cost and highly efficient products with large pore volumes, but also effectively control the morphology, composition and internal structure of catalysts. However, its universality may be limited by the requirement for the different equilibrium potentials of two metal/metal ion redox pairs. Moreover, to successfully prepare NMSACs, the selected second metal should exhibit a higher reduction potential than that of the first metal, which leads to limited metal precursor selection.67,80 In addition to the “bottom-up” strategy, the “top-down” method has also demonstrated promise for the practical production of NMSACs. Benefiting from the simple procedure and usage of inexpensive raw materials, high-temperature pyrolysis is one of the most commonly used “top-down” strategies to synthesize atomically dispersed catalysts. However, the agglomeration tendency of metal atoms into nanoclusters or nanoparticles and the generation of complex species at high temperatures are still critical challenges.81,82 To improve the yield, high-energy ball-milling is another simple and effective “top-down” strategy that can control the size of catalysts by tuning the rotation speed of the balls and realize large-scale fabrication, but this approach has some disadvantages, such as contaminant problems, uncontrollable active sites and long reaction time.76,80 As a result, although various “bottom-up” and “top-down” methods have shown great potential for preparing NMSACs, how to simultaneously obtain high metal-loading SACs with controllable properties and reduce the production cost through a simple and universal method remains an issue for their practical applications. There is still a long way to go to develop more advanced technologies to satisfy the industrial production requirements regarding the performance, yield, environmental friendliness, cost and safety.
Table 1 The advantages and disadvantages of various synthetic strategies for NMSACs




Strategies

Disadvantages

• Simple procedure
• Low metal loadings

• Limited control over the location of the active sites on the support surface






Physical vapor deposition (PVD)
• Simple procedure
• Harsh conditions (e.g., a high vacuum environment and expensive equipment)

• Difficult to precisely control the anchoring sites on the solid substrate

• Slow process of ALD






Atomic layer deposition (ALD)
• Useful for the investigation of structure–performance relationships
• Harsh conditions (e.g., a high vacuum environment and expensive equipment)


• Easy to control the loading amount of NMSACs by adjusting the reaction speed and times
• Difficult to precisely control the anchoring sites on the solid substrate

• Slow process of ALD






Photochemical strategy
• Simple and time-saving procedure
• Restricted to specific systems


• More scalable than the ALD technique
• Narrow applicability


• No requirement for extra chemical or physical treatment








High-temperature pyrolysis
• Simple procedure
• Generation of complex species


• Usage of inexpensive raw materials
• Long reaction time and high energy consumption




• Uncontrollable active sites on the support surface






Galvanic replacement
• Short reaction time
• Restriction on universality


• The possibility to obtain high-efficiency and low-cost products with large pore volumes
• High reduction potential


• Low-cost and energy-efficient
• Limited material selection


• Easy to control the morphology, composition and internal structure








A microwave-assisted strategy
• High yield and short reaction time
• Difficult to control the thickness and loading of single metal atoms


• Low cost






Ball-milling
• Simple procedure
• Irregular morphologies of catalysts


• High yield
• Contaminant problem


• Easy to control the size of catalysts
• Long reaction time


• Wide scope of applications
• Uncontrollable active sites on the support surface




3. Characterization of NMSACs
In recent studies, NMSACs comprised of atomically dispersed noble metals on the support material usually exhibit excellent performance for heterogeneous catalysis. Interestingly, single atoms as catalytically active sites have unique properties, which are distinguished from their nanoparticle or nanocluster counterparts in catalysts.83,84 To attain a deep understanding of NMSACs, it is essential to verify the existence of isolated noble metal atoms and investigate the relationship between their structures and performance. A series of advanced characterization techniques have been developed to probe and analyze SACs. Among them, aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy (AC-STEM) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) are most commonly used and will be described in detail in this section. AC-STEM, an integrated electron microscopy technique, offers the direct observation of active sites at the atomic level, and then a spectroscopic technique XAS is applied to provide more in-depth information (e.g., structural features and the coordination environment) of SACs. Except for these characterization techniques, other effective techniques such as scanning tunneling microscopy (STM), nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy, Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) also play an essential role in investigating the properties of atomic-level catalytically active sites.85–90
3.1. AC-STEM
Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) is a conventional and widely used technique to examine the morphology (shape and size), crystallinity, exposed facets, and surface dispersion of nanomaterials. However, the configuration of single atoms and metal–support interactions are still difficult to be verified by the regular high-resolution TEM (HRTEM).1,91 With the development of aberration correction technology, AC-STEM is capable of identifying the active sites at the atomic scale owing to the added beam current and resolution.92 Particularly, aberration-corrected high-angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) can provide atomic resolution images by collecting the electrons scattered from an annulus around the beam using an annular dark-field detector. As the contrast is related to the atomic number (symbol Z) of detected atoms, this technique allows single metal atoms (especially heavy metal atoms with higher Z) anchored on supports with light elements to be identified directly by observing the atomic-scale bright spots.93,94 For instance, isolated Pt atoms uniformly distributed on different supports including ZrO2, CeO2, Co3O4 and graphene are clearly observed from aberration-corrected HAADF-STEM images (Fig. 4a–h) with obvious contrast. No Pt NPs or clusters can be visualized in all these samples at both low and high magnification, further confirming the successful synthesis of Pt SACs.95
 Fig. 4  AC-HAADF-STEM images of (a and b) Pt1/Co3O4, (c and d) Pt1/CeO2, (e and f) Pt1/ZrO2, and (g and h) Pt1/graphene at both low and high magnification. Reproduced with permission.95 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. (i) Pt L3-edge XANES spectra and (j) Fourier transform (FT) radial distribution function of the Pt L3-edge k3-weighted EXAFS spectra for Pt1/R-Mn2O3-5D, PtO2 and Pt foil. Reproduced with permission.101 Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH. (k) Wavelet transform (WT) for HNTM-Pt (Pt), H2PtCl6, PtO2 and Pt foil. Reproduced with permission.102 Copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH. 
To further probe the structure and chemical composition of SACs, energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) and electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) are the two important material analysis techniques to combine with HAADF-STEM imaging. Due to the high detection limit, EDX is usually applied to characterize SACs with high metal loadings. Compared with the EDX technique, EELS can offer elemental identification with an enhanced resolution, signal-to-noise ratio, and sensitivity to lower Z elements.9,85,96 For example, the existence of single Pt atoms on a defective graphene surface can be confirmed by the bright spots in HAADF-STEM image. The relatively high Z value of Pt species in the catalysts can lead to excellent image contrast. Moreover, the corresponding EELS spectrum and mapping verify that the isolated Pt atoms are coordinated with the neighboring defective carbon instead of N species, as N atoms are not detected around Pt active sites.74
3.2. XAS
In addition to microscopy techniques for probing the structure and chemical composition of NMSACs in a specific area, spectroscopic techniques with higher resolution are commonly applied to further investigate the electronic and coordination structures of atomic active sites considering the entire architecture. Generally, XAS has been considered as one of the typical and powerful spectroscopic techniques for characterizing SACs.4,90,97 This synchrotron-radiation-based tool is composed of two important techniques: X-ray absorption near-edge spectroscopy (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS). XANES can provide detailed information on the geometrical structure and valence state of single atoms. The core electrons are excited to free orbitals in the low energy range of 5–150 eV during the characterization process. In contrast, EXAFS is effective for determining the bond lengths, neighboring atomic species and coordination numbers of the central metal atoms. In this analysis, the electron waves are backscattered from adjacent atoms around the central atom in the high kinetic energy region of 150–2000 eV.4,31,98 It is worth mentioning that the Fourier transform (FT) is a basic step to analyze the coordination environment of central atoms in EXAFS data processing.99,100 Recently, Yan and co-workers investigated the coordination environment of isolated Pt atoms anchored on an Mn2O3 (Pt1/R-Mn2O3-5D) support by applying XANES and EXAFS in the characterization. The XANES spectra (Fig. 4i) reveal that the near-edge absorption energy of atomic Pt is situated at that of PtO2, further confirming that the valence state of atomic Pt is +4 in the catalysts. There are a lot of unoccupied Pt 5d states for Pt ions and oxygen around Pt4+. The FT k3-weighted EXAFS spectra determine the coordination configuration of Pt species in Pt1/R-Mn2O3-5D. No obvious Pt–Pt coordination peak can be observed in Fig. 4j, revealing that Pt atoms exist in an atomically dispersed state in the catalysts. Additionally, the two main peaks located at around 1.60 Å and 2.81 Å are attributed to Pt–O and Pt–Mn bonds, respectively. The strong interaction between high-valent single Pt atoms and the Mn2O3 support can effectively hinder the aggregation of Pt atoms in the synthesis.101 Taking atomically dispersed Ir and Pt anchored on a hollow nanotube MOF (HNTM-Ir/Pt) as another example, more powerful wavelet-transform EXAFS (WT-EXAFS) was employed to analyze the NMSACs. Firstly, the positively charged Pt atoms in the structure can be confirmed in the Pt K-edge XANES spectra, as the white line peak is located between H2PtCl6 and Pt foil. In addition, the absence of detectable Pt–Pt coordination in WT-EXAFS verifies the sole existence of single Pt atoms in HNTM-Ir/Pt. As seen in Fig. 4k, the WT contour plots of the H2PtCl6 and PtO2 standards display two intensity maxima at 8.2 Å−1 and 6.2 Å−1, which correspond to Pt–Cl and Pt–O contributions, respectively. In contrast, only one intensity maximum at 7.2 Å−1 ascribed to Pt–N/Cl is seen in the plot of HNTM-Ir/Pt, which further confirms the atomically dispersed Pt sites.102
3.3. Other techniques
Besides the above-mentioned main characterization techniques, various other advanced techniques could further provide more key information for understanding the working mechanisms of SACs in the catalytic processes.

STM. 
STM, another efficient surface-science technique, is applied for directly observing the atomic-level surface structure of catalysts based on the tunnel effect at the quantum scale. By manipulating the STM conducting tips mechanically, the atomic sites on the surface can be routinely imaged through the recording of the tunneling current, thereby affording more information on the atomic and electronic structure.14,81,103,104 As shown in Fig. 5a and b, STM images employed by Therrien and co-workers reveal the presence of symmetrical CO–Pt units in the structure, confirming that CO is bound to single Pt atoms after CO addition and a 250 K annealing process. In another series of STM images, the atomic-level surface structure change around Pt atoms can be observed during a low-temperature CO oxidation reaction. When annealed at 160 K, Fig. 5c indicates that CO desorbed from the support is absorbed at Pt sites, which is bound to different positions on the surface. Then, in Fig. 5d, an observable location change of CO–Pt and the formation of new defects in the oxide near the Pt sites could be found on the ‘29’ copper oxide when annealed at 250 K. The enhanced mobility of CO–Pt and increased defects could be attributed to the CO adsorption to atomic Pt sites and lattice oxygen removal at 250 K, respectively. When the annealing temperature increased to 350 K, the unchanged area density reveals that Pt is still highly dispersed on the surface. However, compared to Fig. 5d, the promotion of CO2 formation at higher temperatures could lead to further changes in the Pt occupation sites and the generation of more defects adjacent to Pt atoms as shown in Fig. 5e.46 At present, the development of the STM technique is still hindered by the strict working conditions and specimen requirements.80
 Fig. 5  (a) High-resolution STM image of 0.5% of a monolayer Pt coverage on the ‘29’ copper oxide after CO addition and annealing at 250 K. (b) The simulated STM image of the DFT model with a CO–Pt complex in each unit cell at −0.5 V. High-resolution STM image of CO-exposed Pt atoms anchored on the ‘29’ copper oxide after the annealing process at (c) 160 K, (d) 250 K and (e) 350 K. Reproduced with permission.46 Copyright 2018, Nature Publishing Group. (f) XPS Pt 4f spectra of Pt1@MIL and Ptn@MIL. (g) Pt L3-edge XANES spectra of Pt1@MIL, Ptn@MIL and Pt foil. Reproduced with permission.106 Copyright 2019, Nature Publishing Group. (h) FTIR spectra of 0.1Pt/C12A7 before and after exposure to D2. Reproduced with permission.108 Copyright 2020, Nature Publishing Group. (i) The 27Al MAS-NMR spectra of 0.2Pt/m–Al2O3–O2, 0.2Pt/m–Al2O3–H2 and com-Pt/Al2O3. Reproduced with permission.110 Copyright 2017, Nature Publishing Group. 


XPS. 
As a powerful quantitative spectroscopic technique, XPS has been frequently applied for evaluating the elemental composition and the valence states on the surface of catalysts. However, this technique suffers from relatively low atomic resolution and surface limitations, especially under low metal loadings. To better analyze the probed atoms at the atomic level, XPS is usually employed in combination with other techniques (e.g., HAADF-STEM and XAS).14,80,85,90 For instance, the valence state and coordination environment of Pt atoms in Pt1/NMC were investigated by XPS and XANES. The positive binding energy shift of Pt 4f in Pt1/NMC, which is similar to the Pt 4f shift from the bulk state to single Pt atoms, indicates the strong interaction between the Pt sites and the NMC substrate. Then, in the XANES spectra, the white-line intensity of isolated Pt atoms on NMC can be observed to increase in comparison to Pt foil and Pt NPs, further demonstrating the oxidation of Pt atoms in the catalyst.105 In another study, XPS (Fig. 5f) and XANES (Fig. 5g) reveal that the oxidation state of Pt species is identified to be +2 in Pt1@MIL, which is higher than the metallic state of Pt in Ptn@MIL.106


FTIR. 
FTIR is a powerful technique for determining the exact structure of supported metal catalysts, which involves the direct monitoring of the adsorption behaviors of probe guest molecules such as CO and NH3 on the support surface. According to the changes in vibrational frequency and intensity of the adsorbed molecules, the characteristics of the active centers and local structure can be inferred through proper calibration. Generally, the time- and temperature-resolved FTIR technique can be employed to evaluate the presence of atomically dispersed metal sites on the support quantitatively and detect the intermediate species in the catalytic process, which produces more evidence that metal NPs did not exist in SACs.30,82,107 For example, atomically dispersed Ptδ+ species stabilized on the surface of a 12CaO·7Al2O3 (C12A7) support can be confirmed by the CO adsorption behavior revealed by the FTIR technique, as only one peak located at 2070 cm−1 is observed for the 0.1Pt/C12A7 and 0.1Pt/C12A7-R600 samples. In addition, FTIR can also become a reliable tool for elucidating the reaction mechanism. The detectable O–D vibration on 0.1Pt/C12A7 exposed to D2 (instead of 0.1Pt/CaO or 0.1Pt/Al2O3) confirms the cleavage of O–H bonds in the rate-controlling step of the hydrogenation reaction on 0.1Pt/C12A7 (Fig. 5h).108 Compared with the AC-STEM and XAS techniques, FTIR is easy to use in most laboratories to characterize NMSACs, and make up for the shortcomings of insufficient local field of vision. In addition, FTIR spectroscopy (CO probing) is atomically sensitive due to the very strong IR signal and the high sensitivity to the geometry, chemical state and degree of coordination unsaturation of the adsorption site. Thus, although this technique is easily affected by limitations including indirectly acquired information and high sensitivity to organic impurities, FTIR with high sensitivity has a greater advantage in detecting low precious metal loadings in NMSACs.85,109


NMR. 
Another technique for detecting isolated metal atoms and analyzing their coordination environment is NMR spectroscopy, which is usually performed under in situ conditions.30,82 For example, NMR was employed to investigate the binding ligands of isolated Pt atoms in samples of 0.2Pt/m–Al2O3–H2 and 0.2Pt/m–Al2O3–O2. By applying 27Al magic-angle spinning nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy (MAS NMR), three main peaks situated at 7, 38, and 70 ppm can be clearly observed in the spectra (Fig. 5i), demonstrating the existence of AlO4, AlO5 and AlO6 coordination in the structure.110 In characterization, the operation of NMR spectroscopy may suffer from problems including high sensitivity to impurities and susceptibility to magnetism.85,109 Despite these disadvantages, this technique is still effective for investigating the formation mechanism of NMSACs with low metal loadings. The NMR sensitivity can be improved by new hyperpolarization techniques and parahydrogen (p-H2) involved in the catalytic processes. Therefore, high-sensitivity NMR can provide an in-depth understanding of the nature of the atomic active centers under operating conditions.78,79,82,109

4. Catalytic applications of NMSACs
The catalytic applications of NMSACs are versatile. Different from their metal NP/cluster counterparts, noble metal single atoms possess unusual catalytic behaviors including a high density of exposed active sites, robust catalytic activity and selectivity, and excellent stability towards a range of catalytic reactions. Based on the mode of energy supply, they can be classified into thermocatalysis, electrocatalysis, and photocatalysis.4,26,107 In terms of thermocatalysis, NMSACs are able to catalyze a series of key reactions such as CO oxidation, water–gas shift reactions and selective hydrogenation reactions, which are beneficial to facilitate the production of important industrial chemicals and address some environmental issues.4 As for electrocatalysis, a variety of elementary reactions involved in electrochemical energy conversion and storage (such as the ORR, OER, HER and CO2RR) can also be effectively promoted by NMSACs, enabling clean energy conversion processes.57,80 To achieve efficient solar-to-chemical energy conversion, single-atom noble metal photocatalysts are developed and applied in many photocatalytic reactions, including photocatalytic H2 evolution, CO2 reduction and N2 fixation.103 In the following section, recent advances in catalytic applications of NMSACs are summarized and introduced. Specifically, the structure–activity relationships and the catalytic mechanisms were also discussed based on both experimental and computational results, which shed light on designing highly efficient catalysts for future study.
4.1. Thermocatalysis
Thermocatalysis, one of the most well-known catalytic categories, is commonly applied in conventional industrial processes (e.g. the water–gas shift reaction and ammonia synthesis) owing to the high efficiency and applicability for large-scale production.111 Generally, many common heterogeneous catalysts such as zeolites, metals and metal oxides participate in thermally driven catalysis, but the poor stability of catalysts and complex product distribution may hinder their practical applications.4 In recent years, NMSACs have been developed for thermocatalysis from economic and environmental perspectives. Benefiting from advantages including the uniform atomic active sites and unique chemical properties of single atoms, as well as high activity and thermal stability of noble metals, atomically dispersed noble metal catalysts are conducive to accelerating conversion reactions and improving the selectivity to specific products.82,85 In this case, recent studies on a series of thermocatalytic applications of NMSACs are discussed and also summarized in Table 2 to demonstrate their great potential for use as thermocatalysts.

4.1.1. CO oxidation. 
With a significant role in catalysis research and environmental protection, CO oxidation has attracted intensive attention in industrial processes. Owing to the cheap price and easy operation, low-temperature CO oxidation is the most simple and effective approach to eliminate and utilize CO gas.112,113 At present, the investigations of CO oxidation mainly focus on the optimization of the preparation methods and catalytic performance of catalysts, which are advantageous in terms of designing industrially applicable catalysts. It has been well established that SACs are an active research frontier with the maximum atom utilization efficiency and novel catalytic performance. Among them, NMSACs have made great progress in catalyzing CO oxidation in recent years.17,95 For instance, single Pt atoms anchored on the defects of an iron oxide (FeOx) surface (denoted as Pt1/FeOx) were fabricated by Qiao and co-workers using a simple co-precipitation strategy in 2011. Compared with the standard Au/Fe2O3 as a reference, Pt1/FeOx exhibits a higher specific reaction rate of 0.435 molCO h−1 gPt−1 at 27 °C for CO oxidation. The turnover frequency (TOF), defined as the number of molecules reacting on the catalytically active sites per unit time, is an important parameter for comparing the efficiency of active sites between catalytic materials.114,115 The TOF value of Pt1/FeOx (0.136 s−1) is also higher than that of the standard Au/Fe2O3. The superior activity is attributed to the effective electron transfer from isolated Pt atoms to the FeOx support based on Bader charge analysis. The high oxidation state of single Pt atoms coordinated with three O atoms on the surface can lead to lower adsorption energy (1.96 eV) for CO in comparison to Pt10 clusters (3.05 eV), indicating that the individual Pt sites are highly effective for enhancing the CO oxidation activity.19 In addition, noble metal single atoms supported on other metal oxide supports have also been investigated for CO oxidation. In 2017, Zhang et al. adopted a facile modified sol–gel solvent vaporization self-assembly strategy to prepare mesoporous Al2O3 supported Pt SACs with excellent catalytic activity and stability. The sole existence of single Pt atoms is confirmed by the TEM image and corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping as no Pt NPs can be observed (Fig. 6a–c). The only predominant shell of Pt–O situated at approximately 1.7 Å in the EXAFS spectrum also proves the single-atom form of Pt (Fig. 6d). In the synthesis, the P123 template plays a significant role in generating the mesoporous structure with rich unsaturated pentahedral Al3+ centres. The strong interaction between Pt and the support precursors ensures that the low valent Pt atoms in coordination with four oxygen atoms are stabilized in the inner surface of mesoporous Al2O3 (Fig. 6e), resulting in long durations and superior activity of the catalysts. During the CO oxidation process, the TOF value steadily increases from 0.023 s−1 at 200 °C to 0.175 s−1 at 250 °C. Besides, when the temperature is fixed at 400 °C, the conversion of CO can be maintained at 100% for 220 h.110 More recently, Zhao and co-workers prepared single Au atoms supported on CeO2 (Au loading: 0.03 wt%) by an electrostatic adsorption strategy. For comparison, the reference Au/CeO2-RR2Ce with an Au loading of 0.98 wt% was also investigated in this study. From AC-HAADF-STEM, only individual Au atoms can be detected in the catalyst with no observable Au NPs or clusters. Interestingly, the addition of H2O demonstrates a better promotion effect for CO oxidation on Au1/CeO2 SACs in comparison to CeO2 supported Au NPs. After adding ∼2 vol% water, the specific reaction rate (3.6 molCO h−1 gAu−1) and TOF (0.2 s−1) of Au1/CeO2 SACs increase, much higher than those of Au/CeO2-RR2Ce (a specific rate of 0.13 molCO h−1 gAu−1 and TOF of 0.036 s−1) at ambient temperature. Moreover, the bare CeO2 support has little effect on CO oxidation even with the addition of H2O, confirming the unique promotion effect of H2O on single Au atoms. According to DFT calculations, the single Au atoms with positive charge and more variable oxidation states can provide an efficient channel for the (CO + OH) reaction route by adding H2O and –OH groups.116
 Fig. 6  (a) The TEM image, (b) TEM-EDS elemental mapping images and (c) HAADF-STEM image of 0.2Pt/m–Al2O3–H2. (d) The FT k3-weighted EXAFS spectra derived from EXAFS, Δ = 3.0–12.0 Å−1. (e) Schematic illustration of a single Pt atom anchored on the m–Al2O3 support surface for 0.2Pt/m–Al2O3–H2. Reproduced with permission.110 Copyright 2017, Nature Publishing Group. (f) Ru K-edge and (g) Pt L3-edge XANES spectra of RuSA-CN620, PtRuSA-CN620, PtRuSA-CN and Ru foil. XPS spectra of (h) Ru 3p, (i) Pt 4f and (j) N 1s for RuSA-CN620, PtSA-CN620, PtRuSA-CN620 and PtRuSA-CN. Reproduced with permission.117 Copyright 2019, The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
In addition to metal oxides, other materials such as zeolites and g-C3N4 can also be selected as the support for stabilizing noble metal single atoms. For example, [Pt(NH3)4]2+ and PtOx complexes with isolated Pt sites supported in zeolite KLTL exhibited great potential for catalyzing CO oxidation in Kistler's study. The initial TOF value of Pt(NH3)42+/KLTL zeolite and PtOx/KLTL zeolite was 0.0038 and 0.012 s−1, respectively, at 423 K. Based on the EXAFS data, the formation of platinum oxo species in PtOx/KLTL zeolite can lead to the increase of the average Pt–O coordination number from 2 to 3 at 2.0 Å, revealing that the oxygen atoms are coordinated with Pt atoms after the oxidation process.41 Moreover, Zhou et al. synthesized neighboring Ru and Pt monomers anchored on g-C3N4 with rich N vacancies for CO oxidation. The Ru and Pt loading in the catalysts is 0.45 wt% and 0.51 wt%, respectively. Both the XANES (Fig. 6f and g) and XPS results (Fig. 6h–j) indicate that the electron density of the adjacent Pt monomer can be increased by the synergistic effect of the Ru monomers and N vacancies. DFT simulations further recommend that the formation of Ru–N and Pt–C bonds can effectively stabilize the Pt–Ru monomers in the triangular sub-nanometer cavity created by the N vacancies in the structure. Compared with other structures such as C–Ru–Pt–N, C–Ru–Ru–N and N–Pt–Ru–N, the C–Pt–Ru–N structure shows the lowest standard deviations of the reaction step energies (0.66 eV), demonstrating the good ability for catalyzing CO oxidation.117 These results suggest that the CO oxidation performance of NMSACs can be significantly affected by the support materials, owing to the strong interaction of the isolated metal atoms with the support surface.


4.1.2. Water–gas shift (WGS) reaction. 
The water–gas shift (WGS) reaction (CO + H2O → H2 + CO2) plays a vital role in industrial hydrogen production for various chemical processes (e.g., fuel cells and ammonia synthesis).113,118 In the past decades, supported precious metal-based catalysts (especially Pt and Au) have been deeply investigated for the WGS reaction due to the outstanding catalytic performance and stability at low temperatures. However, their wide applications could be limited by the scarcity, expensive price, and unsatisfactory utilization efficiency of noble metals. To reduce the usage of noble metals and maintain the high catalytic activity, NMSACs have drawn intensive attention as a new type of WGS catalysts.119,120 For instance, Lin and co-workers constructed a catalyst of isolated Ir atoms anchored on reducible FeOx (denoted as Ir1/FeOx SAC) and investigated its catalytic performance for the WGS reaction. Despite the low Ir loading (0.01 wt%) in the catalyst, the Ir1/FeOx SAC possesses a high reaction rate of 43.4 molCO gIr−1 h−1 at 300 °C as well as a high TOF value of 2.31 s−1, superior to its nanoclusters and nanoparticles, and many reported Au and Pt-based catalysts. The steady-state CO conversions on a series of Ir/FeOx catalysts also reveal that Ir1/FeOx SAC is more active in the WGS reaction (Fig. 7a). The stability of the catalyst can be further investigated by the long-term stability test at 300 °C and AC-STEM characterization. Both the nearly unchanged CO conversion and the uniformly distributed single Ir atoms under 20 h continuous operation confirm the good stability of the Ir1/FeOx SAC. By XANES and H2 temperature-programmed reduction (TPR) analysis, the positively charged Ir single atoms can significantly promote the reduction of the FeOx support, thereby generating more oxygen vacancies as active sites for water dissociation in the WGS process. Benefiting from the synergistic effect between isolated Ir atoms and the reducible FeOx support rich in oxygen vacancies, the Ir1/FeOx SAC exhibits great potential for catalyzing the WGS reaction.120 Besides, an FeOx-supported single Pd atom catalyst (Pd/FeOx) and TiO2-supported single Rh atom catalyst (Rh/TiO2) also confirm the key role of noble metal single atoms in facilitating the generation of oxygen vacancies on reducible supports.121,122
 Fig. 7  (a) CO conversions on a series of Ir/FeOx catalysts at 300 °C. Reproduced with permission.120 Copyright 2013, American Chemical Society. (b) Pt 4f XPS spectra of Pt/1000-2h-CN and Pt1Na6/1000-2h-CN under WGS conditions. Reproduced with permission.125 Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. AC-HAADF-STEM images of Na-promoted single Pt atoms anchored on (c) mesoporous silica MCM-41 ([Si]MCM41), (d) a microporous K-type L-zeolite (KLTL) and (e) high-surface-area anatase TiO2. (f) TOF plot of various Na-containing Pt catalysts on different supports (TiO2, KLTL, [Si]MCM41, SiO2 and CNTs) for the WGS reaction. Reproduced with permission.123 Copyright 2015, American Chemical Society. 
In many previous studies, atomic noble metal species could be stabilized on supports such as titania, iron oxide and ceria through –O ligands. However, it still remained a challenge to eliminate the interference of noble metal NPs in the catalysts at temperatures above ambient, especially on ‘inert’ supports (e.g., silica and zeolites).123 To prepare NMSACs anchored on nonreducible oxides or other oxygen-free supports for the WGS reaction, alkali ion addition is an effective way to stabilize single metal sites along with oxo and hydroxyl groups. For example, single Au atoms (0.25 wt%) on potassium (K) or sodium (Na)-promoted mesoporous MCM-41 silica and KLTL-zeolite were prepared for the WGS reaction at low temperatures (<200 °C) by Yang and co-workers. Based on microscopy studies, the dispersion of Au can be effectively improved by the introduction of alkali metal ions. In situ EXAFS analysis indicates that the atomically dispersed Au species coordinated with alkali ions can result in a significant reduction of the Au–Au coordination number before and after the reaction (from 11 or 12 to 3 or 4), further demonstrating the 100% Au dispersion in the catalysts. By connecting Au–O(OH)x-species with alkali ions through oxygen bonding, the as-formed single-site Au–O(OH)x–(Na or K) ensembles are highly active sites for the WGS reaction.124 The effect of alkali ion addition on the formation of NMSACs has also been confirmed in other studies for the WGS reaction. In the work conducted by Zugic et al., atomically dispersed Pt species can be stabilized on oxygen-free multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs) by the introduction of sodium. As shown in the AC-HAADF-STEM image and particle size distribution, more atomically dispersed Pt species remain on the Na-promoted MWNT support in comparison to the Na-free Pt-based catalyst after the WGS reaction. Further in situ atmospheric-pressure (AP) XPS reveals that the Pt 4f peak positions of the Na-promoted Pt catalyst can be kept constant, while an obvious change in the binding energies can be observed in the peak positions of the Na-free Pt catalyst with reduced temperature (Fig. 7b). This result demonstrates the role of Na in stabilizing active oxidized Pt. The formation of the Pt–Ox–Nay–OHz complex in the presence of Na can contribute to preventing metal agglomeration and promoting the WGS reaction.125
To further investigate the ‘support effect’ for the WGS process, Yang et al. reported single Pt atoms anchored on various sodium (Na)-promoted supports including mesoporous silica, zeolites and TiO2. The single Pt sites in the Na-containing Pt catalysts can be confirmed by the observable atomically dispersed Pt in AC-HAADF-STEM micrographs (Fig. 7c–e). Then, the subsequent XPS measurement indicates that the Pt atoms are coordinated with Na–Ox– on the supports. With the addition of Na, more –OH groups can be generated and bonded to Pt atoms to form active Pt(II)–O(OH)x– species, thereby enhancing the WGS activity in a low temperature range (<150–300 °C). Based on the calculated TOF value of Pt atoms on different supports (Fig. 7f), similar results reveal the indirect ‘support effect’ in the WGS process.123 Therefore, the catalytic behaviors of NMSACs rely heavily on the interaction between the atomically dispersed noble metal sites and support surface, which can be greatly modified by the presence of –OH groups. Notably, alkali ion addition plays a vital role in promoting the generation of –OH groups and the stabilization of atomic metal species.


4.1.3. Selective hydrogenation. 
Selective catalytic hydrogenation is a fundamental reaction in the production of various chemicals such as polymers, agrochemicals, fragrances and pharmaceutical compounds for academia and industry.107,126 From the perspectives of the economy and environment, the key to the selective hydrogenation of unsaturated compounds is to improve the selectivity by fabricating highly efficient catalysts. Generally, compared with homogeneous catalysts, heterogeneous catalysts are more commonly utilized for the reaction due to the easy separation and recovery of the catalysts from the products.28,127 In recent years, NMSACs (e.g., Pt, Pd and Au) with uniform atomically dispersed active sites and unique chemical properties have been investigated as a new generation of catalysts. Based on previous research, NMSACs can provide a lower H2 adsorption energy and facilitate H2 activation in comparison to their nanoclusters or nanoparticles, thereby exhibiting superior catalytic activity and selectivity in selective hydrogenation reactions.107,128
Hydrogenation of nitroarenes. 
Hydrogenation of nitroarenes is one of the important subclasses of selective hydrogenation reactions, which involves the production of aromatic amines (e.g., anilines and chloroaniline) or azobenzene in the presence of molecular hydrogen.14 In the work conducted by Wei et al., isolated Pt atoms anchored on FeOx exhibit high selectivity up to 99% for the hydrogenation of 3-nitrostyrene, superior to the previously reported Pt-based catalysts. In addition, the Pt/FeOx SAC produces a TOF of approximately 1500 h−1, which is about 20 times higher than the reported best results. The remarkable catalytic performance can be ascribed to the following aspects. The positively charged Pt sites and absence of Pt–Pt bonds determined by XAFS techniques are conducive to the favorable adsorption of nitro groups. Moreover, the electron transfer from isolated Pt atoms to the FeOx support attributed to the metal–support interaction also plays an essential role in the chemoselective reduction of nitroarenes.129 Despite the superior catalytic activity per Pt atom, the ultra-low metal loading (0.08 wt%) may hinder the practical application of the Pt/FeOx SAC. Therefore, based on previous research, Wei and co-workers further developed a Pt/FeOx SAC promoted by alkali metals (e.g., Na+, K+ and Li+) with a high Pt loading of 2.16 wt%. After the addition of Na, the formation of Pt–O–Na–O–Fe– species on the surface is helpful to prevent Pt atom agglomeration and produce more positively charged isolated Pt centers for –NO2 group adsorption. The significant positive effect of alkali ions on the catalyst opens up a new route for preparing high-loading NMSACs in selective hydrogenation reactions.130 In another study, Ye et al. reported that single Pt atoms on 12CaO·7Al2O3 (C12A7) crystals yield a high TOF value of 25772 h−1, which is the highest among all the reported Pt group materials (Fig. 8a). Nanoporous C12A7 is an ideal substrate for stabilizing single atoms owing to its unique cavity nanostructure and positive charge. Compared with CaO and Al2O3 as support materials, the desorption of the aniline product is much faster from C12A7. Moreover, the up-shift of the d-band states of Pt atoms on C12A7 can lead to a strong adsorption capability for reactants (e.g., nitroarenes and H2). Therefore, the synergistic effect between the atomically dispersed Pt and the support surface plays a key role in fabricating stable NMSACs and enhancing the catalytic performance for selective hydrogenation and other catalytic reactions.108

Reproduced with permission.132 Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society.
