(h) Schematic illustration of the butene selectivity improvement on Pd1/graphene. Reproduced with permission.50 Copyright 2015, American Chemical Society. 
Semihydrogenation of acetylene to ethylene. 
In addition to the hydrogenation of nitroarenes, NMSACs also demonstrate highly selective catalysis of semihydrogenation of acetylene to ethylene (C2H2 + H2 → C2H4). For industrial polyethylene production, removing trace amounts of acetylene impurities from ethylene feedstock is a vital step to avoid poisoning of the catalysts. Pd-Based catalysts have been found to be efficient for acetylene hydrogenation; however, achieving high ethylene selectivity and preventing the undesired reaction of ethylene to ethane still remain a challenge due to the strong adsorption of ethylene on the active Pd sites.12,131,132 To address the above problems, many previous studies have achieved high selectivity toward ethylene by employing catalysts containing Pd single atoms or SAAs. For example, Li's group prepared single Pd atoms stabilized on N-doped carbon (Pd–SAs) by the carbonization of a Pd NP@ZIF-8 composite. Compared with the Pd NP sample, the Pd–SAs exhibit a superior acetylene conversion of 96.0% with a higher ethylene selectivity of 93.4% at 120 °C (Fig. 8b and c). Further DFT calculations indicate that the molecular collision possibility between hydrogen and C2H2 can be increased on the Pd–N4 structure in the Pd–SAs as the hydrogen prefers to occupy the N sites while C2H2 prefers the isolated Pd sites (Fig. 8d and e). Therefore, Pd SACs have been confirmed to be highly efficient in selective hydrogenation reactions.133 Apart from Pd single atoms, Cu-alloyed single-atom Pd anchored on silica gel was reported to achieve 100% acetylene conversion, as well as approximately 85% selectivity to ethylene. As revealed in EXAFS spectra, the shorter distance in the first shell compared to Pd foil and the sole presence of Pd–Cu bonds in CuPd0.006/SiO2 suggest CuPd alloy formation and the isolation of Pd atoms by Cu when the atomic ratio of Pd to Cu is 0.006. Then, a microcalorimetric measurement and DFT calculations were conducted to further investigate the origin of the catalytic performance (Fig. 8f). The weak adsorption of ethylene and promotion of H2 dissociation over the Cu-alloyed Pd SAC can be ascribed to the complete isolation of Pd atoms and efficient electron transfer from Cu to Pd, thereby exhibiting excellent catalytic activity and selectivity for the semihydrogenation reaction of acetylene to ethylene.132
Other selective hydrogenation reactions. 
Besides, NMSACs can also be efficient for hydrogenation of various chemicals such as quinoline, 1,3-butadiene and phenylacetylene. For instance, Wang and co-workers employed a coordination-assisted strategy using UiO-66-NH2 hosted-RuCl3 as the precursor to prepare atomically dispersed Ru supported on N-doped porous carbon (denoted as Ru SA/N–C). In the chemoselective hydrogenation of quinoline, Ru SA/N–C demonstrates a high py-THQ selectivity of over 99%, superior to N-doped carbon-supported Ru NPs (79%) and commercial Ru/C (9.0%). With the assistance of the free amine groups, Ru ions can be stabilized as isolated sites by forming strong coordination on the support, resulting in excellent selectivity toward py-THQ.42 In addition, as reported by Lu's group, single Pd atoms on a graphene support fabricated by the ALD strategy give 100% selectivity to butenes at about 50 °C with a TOF value of 0.35 s−1 at 47 °C in the hydrogenation of 1,3-butadiene (Fig. 8g). No visible aggregation of metal atoms or activity/selectivity decline after 100 h operation on stream is seen, further confirming the excellent durability of the Pd SAC. The outstanding catalytic performance can be attributed to the changes of the adsorption mode for 1,3-butadiene and enhancement of steric effects on the isolated Pd sites (Fig. 8h).50 In recent years, the selective hydrogenation of phenylacetylene has also been well-investigated as one of the important hydrogenation reactions in industry. For instance, a single-atom Pd catalyst on nanoscale TiO2 synthesized by a self-assembly strategy can achieve a high styrene selectivity of 91% at almost 100% phenylacetylene conversion, as well as a TOF of more than 8000 h−1. Owing to the atomically dispersed active Pd sites, and nanoscale TiO2 with high surface area and unique electronic structure, the as-prepared Pd SAC exhibits better catalytic performance in comparison to the TiO2-supported Pd SAC prepared by the impregnation method and commercial 10% Pd/C.128 These investigations suggest that the selection of the appropriate noble metal element and conductive substrate in the design of NMSACs is of vital importance for a specific selective hydrogenation reaction.


4.1.4. Dehydrogenation and reforming reactions. 
Dehydrogenation and reforming reactions are widely used in industry, involving the critical steps of X–H bond cleavage and hydrogen molecule recombination.134
Dehydrogenation reactions. 
In terms of dehydrogenation reactions, the production of many industrial chemicals has already been realized, such as dehydrogenation of propane to propylene, formaldehyde production via methanol dehydrogenation, and dehydrogenation of ethanol to hydrogen and acetaldehyde. Due to the reversible endothermicity of the dehydrogenation process, the deactivation of the catalyst under harsh conditions may become a problem. Therefore, it is highly desirable to prepare a catalyst with high stability and selectivity at high temperatures and optimize the reaction conditions through various effective methods. Recently, Pt-based materials have been reported to be promising for heterogeneous catalysis. In particular, Pt SACs and SAAs have drawn great attention owing to their exceptional catalytic properties.135,136 For example, Sun et al. prepared γ-alumina-supported Pt/Cu SAA catalysts by an atom dilution method for propane dehydrogenation at high temperatures. When the Pt loading is 0.1 wt%, Pt/Cu displays approximately 90% selectivity toward propylene as well as a high formation rate of 10.6 mol gPt−1 h−1 under atmospheric pressure at 520 °C. As suggested by DFT calculations, the exceptional catalytic performance of atomically dispersed Pt on Cu NPs can be attributed to the appropriate propane dehydrogenation activation barrier and weak binding strength of propylene bonded to the surface. Compared with isolated Pt atoms supported on Ag NPs, the more exposed Pt atoms on the Cu surface can lead to high Pt atom utilization efficiency, thereby enhancing the catalytic activity. Moreover, the prepared Pt/Cu SAA catalyst can maintain excellent stability on stream at high temperatures for more than 120 h, demonstrating its practical application potential in industry.136 Apart from propane dehydrogenation, Pt SAAs were also reported to be efficient for hydrolytic dehydrogenation of ammonia borane (AB) (NH3BH3 + 2H2O → NH4+ + BO2− + 3H2). In 2017, Li and co-workers successfully synthesized Pt/Ni SAAs composed of atomically dispersed Pt on Ni particles, which could exhibit superb catalytic performance in hydrolytic dehydrogenation of AB to hydrogen. As confirmed by XAFS and electron microscopy (Fig. 9a and b), the atomic Pt sites are uniformly dispersed and surrounded by Ni atoms in the alloys. Further experimental and simulation results reveal that the synergistic effect between the isolated negatively charged Pt atoms and adjacent positively charged Ni atoms can significantly reduce the kinetic barrier in the reaction. Combined with the effect of the active Ni-surrounded Pt center, the Pt/Ni SAAs achieve a high TOF value of 12000 molH2 molPt−1 min−1, which is approximately 21 times as high as the best Pt-based catalysts reported so far.71 Besides, other dehydrogenation reactions such as selective formic acid dehydrogenation and non-oxidative ethanol dehydrogenation have also been investigated in the past few years. For instance, Pt–Cu SAAs prepared by physical vapor deposition can reach a high yield of formic acid dehydrogenation toward CO2 and H2. Compared with Cu(111), the superior catalytic performance can be attributed to the lower O–H bond breaking barrier provided by SAAs. In addition, the Pt–Cu SAA catalyst was also reported to be highly efficient for selective dehydrogenation of ethanol to acetaldehyde and H2 by Sykes's group, implying the great potential of Pt SAAs for dehydrogenation and other heterogeneous catalysis.45
 Fig. 9  (a) Normalized Pt L3-edge XANES spectra of 5% Ni/CNT samples with different Pt amounts. (b) The FT k3-weighted EXAFS spectra derived from EXAFS, Δk = 3–10 Å−1. Reproduced with permission.71 Copyright 2017, American Chemical Society. (c) Methanol conversion and CO2 selectivity of Au1/ZnO, Pt1/ZnO and ZnO-nws as a function of reaction time at 390 °C (left). The corresponding Arrhenius plots of Ln(TOF) (s−1) versus 1/T for the MSR reaction (right). Reproduced with permission.37 Copyright 2014, American Chemical Society. Reproduced with permission.138 Copyright 2017, Nature Publishing Group. 
Reforming reactions. 
It has been reported that Pt SACs and SAAs can also efficiently catalyze some reforming reactions, which are the easiest approach to generate hydrogen and syngas from light hydrocarbons (e.g., methanol and ethanol).137 In 2014, isolated Pt and Au atoms anchored on a ZnO nanowire surface (denoted as Pt1/Au1/ZnO) were fabricated by Gu et al. for methanol steam reforming (MSR). Owing to the strong synergistic effect between single Pt/Au atoms and the coordinated oxygen atoms on ZnO, the enhanced binding toward intermediates at stable single active sites can efficiently lower the reaction barriers and even change the reaction routes, further resulting in the outstanding catalytic activity of the Pt1/Au1/ZnO catalyst for the MSR reaction. Specifically, the TOF measured at the isolated Pt sites is over 1000 times as high as the value of pristine ZnO (Fig. 9c).37 In addition to traditional steam methanol reforming at high temperatures (200–350 °C), the aqueous-phase reforming of methanol (APRM) has also been developed to reduce the required energy and achieve a more facile and simpler design for polymer electrolyte membrane fuel cell (PEMFC) applications. Recently, Lin and co-workers reported that atomically dispersed Pt was stabilized on α-molybdenum carbide (α-MoC) taking advantage of the strong interaction between Pt and α-MoC. Notably, when the Pt loading decreases to 0.2%, the 0.2% Pt/α-MoC catalyst with dominant single Pt atoms possesses an exceptional H2 production rate and stability, as well as an excellent TOF value of 18046 h−1 in the APRM process at low temperatures. Such remarkable catalytic performance can be ascribed to the high density of electron-deficient active Pt sites for methanol adsorption and activation, and high water-dissociation activity on the α-MoC substrate (Fig. 9d and e).138 In short, the development of Pt-based SACs and SAAs paves the way to design cost-effective and efficient catalysts for industrial dehydrogenation and reforming reactions.


4.1.5. Other reactions. 
With the rapid development of SACs in recent years, NMSACs have been investigated and applied for other thermal catalytic reactions such as NO oxidation, hydroformylation reactions and oxidation of alcohols. For example, DeBusk's group found that atomically dispersed Pt supported on a θ-Al2O3 substrate was highly efficient for NO oxidation. Previous studies reported that the NO oxidation activity decreases with reduced particle size on the basis of a size-dependent effect. However, the comparable TOF value between supported single-atom Pt and fully formed large-size Pt particles reveals the great potential of small-size single Pt atoms on inert θ-Al2O3 for facilitating NO oxidation. Unlike Pt NPs, the pathway for the oxidation reaction on isolated Pt sites is the modified Langmuir–Hinshelwood (L–H) scheme (Fig. 10a), which does not require the dissociative oxygen adsorption step.139 Besides, Wang and co-workers reported that a single-atom Rh catalyst on a CoO nanosheet support exhibits a remarkable butyraldehyde selectivity of 94.4% and superior TOF of 2065 h−1 in comparison to other reported Rh SACs for the hydroformylation reaction (Fig. 10b). Based on DFT calculations (Fig. 10c–e), the reconstruction of active single Rh sites in Rh/CoO can lead to propene adsorption enhancement and strong interactions between CO and Rh atoms. Owing to the enhanced adsorption and activation of reactants in the co-adsorbed configurations, the Rh/CoO SAC shows excellent catalytic performance under the operation conditions of the hydroformylation reaction.69 In previous literature on the selective aerobic oxidation of allylic alcohols, a mesoporous Al2O3-supported single-atom Pd catalyst (denoted as Pd/meso-Al2O3) was reported to be the most active catalyst with an excellent TOF value of 4400 h−1 and outstanding aldehyde selectivity of over 91%. Compared with conventional γ-Al2O3, the mesoporous Al2O3 support with high surface area shows a better ability to stabilize the isolated Pd sites and promote the Pd atom dispersion, confirming the significant role of substrate optimization in enhancing the catalytic performance. Combined with the simple synthesis and excellent longevity, the Pd/meso-Al2O3 catalyst that contains highly dispersed single Pd centers is responsible for the exceptional catalytic activity and selectivity in the oxidation of alcohols.140 In summary, based on both experimental research and practical applications, the current achievement of NMSACs opens up a door for the rational design of highly efficient catalysts for thermocatalytic applications.
 Fig. 10  (a) NO oxidation pathways on single-atom Pt anchored on the (010) surface of θ-Al2O3. Reproduced with permission.139 Copyright 2014, Nature Publishing Group. (b) Product yield and TOF of 0.2% Rh/CoO, 1.0% Rh/CoO and 4.8% Rh/CoO for hydroformylation of propene after 2 h at 100 °C. (c) The model of Rh1/CoO including the top view of the adsorption configurations of H2, CO, and propene on Rh1/CoO, and the top view of the co-adsorbed configuration of both H2 and CO, as well as four co-adsorbed configurations of the reactants and their corresponding adsorption energies. 4.2. Electrocatalysis
As is well known, electrocatalysis plays a crucial role in many energy-conversion processes and industrial applications. Among a range of rationally designed catalysts, supported NMSACs have received great interest in recent years as a result of the robust intrinsic catalytic activity, high density of exposed active sites and excellent stability during electrocatalytic reactions.4,26,40 Thus, the electrochemical applications of atomically dispersed noble metal catalysts in various critical energy-conversion reactions (e.g., the ORR, OER, HER, CO2RR and NRR) are summarized in the following section (Table 3). Moreover, the relationship between the unique structure of single atoms and their performance is also discussed to demonstrate great promise of NMSACs for electrocatalysis.

4.2.1. Oxygen reduction reaction (ORR). 
Proton exchange membrane fuel cells (PEMFCs) and metal–air batteries have emerged as promising electrochemical energy-conversion technologies owing to the high efficiency and zero emissions. In the operating process, the electrocatalytic ORR occurs at the cathode to convert chemical energy into electrical energy.81,209,210 Generally, the reduction of oxygen may follow either a two-electron (O2 + 2H+ + 2e− → H2O2) or a four-electron pathway (O2 + 4H+ + 4e− → 2H2O) depending on the preservation of the O–O bond during the ORR process. The former two-electron route is a facile and energy-saving synthetic pathway for producing the important industrial fine chemical hydrogen peroxide (H2O2), while the latter involving direct water generation is preferred for energy conversion and storage devices due to the high energy-conversion efficiency.29,80,112 Usually, Pt-based materials are employed as efficient electrocatalysts to overcome the sluggish kinetics of the ORR. However, the supply scarcity and high cost could severely limit their large-scale commercialization. Therefore, it is highly desirable to develop low-cost and high-performance catalysts as alternatives to the traditional Pt-based catalysts. Recently, many single noble metal atoms (e.g., Pt, Pd and Ru) supported on conductive carbon substrates have been reported to be effective catalysts for the ORR owing to their maximum atom utilization efficiency of noble metals and excellent stability.85,211
ORR via the 2e− pathway. 
For the two-electron reduction pathway, the reaction occurring at the atomic sites can effectively preserve the O–O bond, further resulting in high selectivity to H2O2.212 In 2016, the Lee group employed the incipient wetness impregnation method to prepare isolated Pt atoms on TiN NPs with the assistance of chlorine ligands. The formed Pt SACs predominantly undergo a two-electron pathway for electrochemical H2O2 production in acidic conditions with a high yield of 90%. Both HAADF-STEM (Fig. 11a) and EXAFS characterization confirm the presence of single Pt atoms in the 0.35 wt% Pt/TiN catalyst, which can be stabilized at the N-vacancy sites on the support according to previous DFT calculations. Compared with TiN-supported Pt NPs, single-atom Pt possesses the more reduced state of Pt and stronger interaction between isolated Pt sites and the TiN support based on XANES and EXAFS analysis. Specifically, the 0.35 wt% Pt/TiN catalyst achieves an outstanding mass activity of 78 A gPt−1 at an overpotential of 0.05 V. Such excellent catalytic activity for H2O2 production can be attributed to the insufficient OO bond breaking on the discontinuous isolated Pt sites. Therefore, this work demonstrates that the atomically dispersed Pt sites prefer to follow a two-electron pathway different from the Pt NPs.213 Furthermore, the role of the support in single-atom Pt catalysts for H2O2 production was also investigated by Lee and co-workers. Compared with Pt/TiN, isolated Pt atoms supported on TiC exhibit higher catalytic activity and selectivity, and better stability owing to the weaker interaction with oxygen species and better preservation of the O–O bond on the TiC surface. Thus, the selection of an appropriate support for anchoring single atoms is significant for designing high-performance catalysts considering the support effect.214 Recently, high-concentration atomically dispersed Pt supported on amorphous CuSx (denoted as h-Pt1–CuSx) was reported to be a promising catalyst for H2O2 synthesis. By adopting the ion-exchange method, the Pt precursor (Pt2+) can be reduced to Pt0 and stabilized on the support surface (Cu1.94S nanoparticles) by forming strong coordination with sulfur. Then, after the oxidation and removement of the interior of Cu1.94S support, abundant isolated Pt sites can be achieved in the hollow structure. Owing to the rich atomic active sites, h-Pt1–CuSx yields an excellent selectivity of 92–96% toward H2O2 in a potential range of 0.05–0.7 V vs reversible hydrogen electrode (RHE) in acidic conditions (Fig. 11b and c), as well as an ultrahigh H2O2 productivity of 546 ± 30 mol kgcat−1 h−1.215 In addition to Pt SACs, other noble metals have also been developed as efficient ORR catalysts for H2O2 production. For instance, Kim et al. prepared a single-atom Pd catalyst stabilized on a C@C3N4 support with the aid of rich N sites by the wet impregnation method. When the Pd loading is 0.5 wt%, the catalyst exhibits an outstanding activity of 8.5 A mgPd−1 at 0.2 V and selectivity up to 94% for H2O2 production. However, a further increase in the Pd loading can lead to a decrease in selectivity as a result of the formation of Pd ensembles. Based on DFT calculations, the superior H2O2 selectivity of C@C3N4–Pd1 compared to C@C3N4 supported single-atom Pt should be attributed to the weaker binding energy of O2 at single Pd sites (−0.99 eV). Thus, Pd SACs can be efficient electrocatalysts for selective production of H2O2.212 In another study, single atom Au–Pd nanoalloys composed of isolated Pd atoms (8% Pd molar content) embedded in Au NPs also result in the two-electron pathway, with a high H2O2 selectivity of up to 95%. Obviously, compared with pure gold and Au–Pd alloys with contiguous Pd ensembles on the surface, the Au as a ‘host’ modified by the individual Pd ‘guest’ atoms exhibits significantly enhanced oxygen reduction toward H2O2, demonstrating the great potential of Au alloyed with isolated transition metal atoms for electrocatalytic H2O2 production.216

(h) Schematic illustration of the ORR reaction pathway on the a(Co–Pt)@N8V4 catalyst. Reproduced with permission.219 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society. 
ORR via the 4e− pathway. 
An efficient 4e− ORR can be achieved by the effective adsorption of O2 molecules on two neighboring active sites for O–O bond breaking. In the past years, many studies reported that Pt NPs with high Pt content could be practical for the ORR toward H2O/OH− in fuel cells and metal–air batteries, while the high cost is still the major bottleneck for their large-scale applications. To reduce the price and improve the performance, reducing the Pt particle size to the atomic level is one of the most effective strategies for improving the Pt atom utilization efficiency. Recent studies demonstrated that N-coordinated and C-coordinated atomic Pt sites prefer to catalyze the ORR via a four-electron pathway.29,217 For example, Liu et al. developed a cheap N-doped carbon black-supported atomically dispersed Pt catalyst with a Pt loading of 0.4 wt% for an efficient four-electron ORR. The formed Pt SAC with N co-doping (denoted as Pt1–N/BP) offers a higher half-wave potential of 0.76 VRHE and 0.87 VRHE than pure BP, N-doped BP and a BP-supported Pt SAC in 0.1 M HClO4 and 0.1 M KOH solutions, respectively, revealing the significant role of the synergistic effect between single-atom Pt and doped-N for enhancing the ORR performance (Fig. 11d and e). Besides, when employing the Pt1–N/BP catalyst at the cathode for acidic H2/O2 fuel cell measurements, a high Pt utilization of 0.13 gPt kW−1 and excellent fuel cell durability could be achieved. Further DFT calculations demonstrate that the main active sites for the 4e− ORR are atomic Pt centers anchored by single pyridinic-N, different from many reported Pt SACs and traditional Pt/C. Therefore, the doping of N on carbon-supported single-atom Pt catalysts sheds light on the design of cost-effective and powerful electrocatalysts for the ORR in the future.218 In addition, the formation of metal atom pairs at the atomic scale on defective carbon was also reported to be a feasible strategy for achieving high selectivity to H2O/OH− in the ORR. In 2018, Zhang and co-workers demonstrated that atomic Pt–Co trapped in the defects of N-doped carbon (A-CoPt–NC) shows outstanding catalytic activity and stability for the 4e− ORR in alkaline conditions, as well as superior specific and mass activities to commercial Pt/C. Compared with the A-CoPt–C catalyst without N doping, the absence of Pt–Pt coordination and no observable Pt NPs in the characterization reveal the important role of N in trapping and stabilizing atomic Pt. According to the DFT calculations (Fig. 11f–h), the synergistic effect between atomic Pt and Co sites can lead to the shift of the d orbitals and proper charge redistribution, thereby greatly enhancing the ORR performance with the four-electron pathway.219 Apart from Pt SACs, other NMSACs such as Au, Ru and Ir also display remarkable catalytic performance for the ORR. An efficient “amine-induced reduction” method was applied by Liu and co-workers to prepare Au1Nx single sites on a graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) support. Owing to the opposite charge, the adsorbed auric chloride group containing Au3+ ions can first combine with the electron donor N species of amine, then reduce to the lower valence state of Au1+ and anchor on the g-C3N4 surface by the formation of an Au–N bond. Characterization by HAADF-STEM shows that the atomic metal sites are uniformly dispersed on the support with the absence of observable metal NPs. Further XPS and EXAFS validate the strong interaction of Au–N bonding for atomic Au1Nx single sites. Compared with the commercial Pt/C, the g-C3N4-supported Au1Nx single-site catalyst shows comparable ORR activity with a half-wave potential of approximately 0.76 V, and superior kinetics with a Tafel slope of 74 mV dec−1 in alkaline conditions.77 Additionally, an Ir-SAC with an Ir–N–C configuration was successfully prepared by Xiao and co-workers using ZIF-8 as a suitable host to encapsulate the guest Ir acetylacetonate. After the high-temperature annealing process, the formed single Ir atoms anchored on the porous nitrogenous carbon deliver a half-wave potential of 0.864 V in acidic electrolyte, outperforming N-doped carbon-supported Ir NPs and many recently reported catalysts for the ORR. Moreover, an exceptional mass activity of 12.2 A mgIr−1 and ultrahigh TOF value of 24.3 e− site−1 s−1 at 0.85 V (vs. RHE) can also be achieved by the Ir-SAC as a result of the optimal adsorption energy of ORR intermediates on the isolated Ir–N4 sites.61 In summary, the surrounding atomic configuration of SACs can significantly influence the catalytic activity and selectivity for the ORR. Based on theoretical calculations, rational design of NMSACs opens up new opportunities for developing highly efficient and durable catalysts with ideal nanostructures and enhanced ORR performance.


4.2.2. Oxygen evolution reaction (OER). 
The OER is a critical half reaction at the anode in many sustainable energy conversion and storage devices such as water electrolyzers and rechargeable metal–air batteries. Owing to the sluggish kinetics of the four-electron transfer process and the requirement for applying large overpotentials in comparison to the cathodic reaction, tremendous efforts have been made to develop highly efficient and stable electrocatalysts for enhancing the energy conversion efficiency.40,220–222 Generally, noble metal-based catalysts such as IrO2 and RuO2 are regarded as the most efficient OER electrocatalysts in terms of the high performance, while the low abundance, high cost and poor long-term stability are the major hindrances to their large-scale application. As such, NMSACs have garnered great attention for the OER because of the unique physiochemical properties and exceptional atom utilization efficiency.223–225
Inspired by the unique and stable properties of M–N–C moieties, Cao and co-workers synthesized a highly efficient and durable nitrogen-carbon-supported single-atom Ru catalyst through the wet impregnation strategy, followed by controlled pyrolysis. Based on EXAFS analysis (Fig. 12a), atomic Ru is successfully coordinated with four neighboring pyridinic-N atoms to form an Ru1–N4 configuration. XPS and NMR characterizations reveal that the partial replacement of C atoms by P atoms can effectively enhance the electrical conductivity without a framework change. In 0.5 M H2SO4 electrolyte, the overpotentials of the Ru–N–C catalyst are 267 and 340 mV at 10 and 100 mA cm−2, respectively, which are much lower than those of RuO2/C. Moreover, the outstanding catalytic performance can be maintained for more than 30 h during continuous operation. It is worth noting that both XAFS (Fig. 12b and c) and synchrotron radiation-based FTIR (SR-FTIR) (Fig. 12d and e) demonstrate that the O–Ru1–N4 structure can be formed by pre-adsorbing single oxygen atoms on Ru1–N4 sites under the working potential, resulting in a reaction barrier reduction, thereby enhancing the OER activity in acidic conditions.226 In another study, atomically dispersed Ru on N-doped graphene (denoted as 0.4-Ru@NG-750) prepared by the high-temperature annealing strategy demonstrates that the stable RuN4(O)Cx structure shows great potential for catalyzing the OER in alkaline conditions. By effectively optimizing the *OH/*O adsorption on the active sites, the 0.4-Ru@NG-750 catalyst possesses superior catalytic performance for the OER in comparison to 0.4-Ru@G-750 and NG.64
 Fig. 12  (a) The R-space curve-fitting of ex situ Ru–N–C. The inset is the structure of the Ru site in Ru–N–C. (b) Operando EXAFS spectra and first-shell fitting curves for Ru–N–C under open circuit conditions and working conditions during the OER process. (c) Operando XANES spectra at the Ru K-edge for Ru–N–C during the OER process. The inset is the magnified pre-edge XANES region. (d) Operando SR-FTIR spectroscopy measurements for Ru–N–C during the OER process in acidic media. (e) The enlarged infrared signal appearing at ∼764 cm−1 in the FTIR spectra. (g) Overpotential at 10 mA cm−2 and Tafel slope for the SAu/NiFe LDH and NiFe LDH. (j) The free energy diagram of the OER on M SAC–CuO (M = Rh, Ir, Au, Ru). (k) Computed free energy diagram of the HER in alkaline conditions. Reproduced with permission.229 Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
Recently, the decoration of other noble metal single atoms such as Au and Ir has also been reported to be efficient for tuning the local electronic structure and boosting the electrocatalytic OER activity of the support materials.227 For instance, Zhang's group developed an NiFe layered double hydroxide (LDH)-supported single-atom Au catalyst with an Au loading of 0.4 wt% through an electrodeposition method. Compared with the NiFe LDH, the Au/NiFe LDH catalyst requires a lower overpotential of 237 mV to achieve 10 mA cm−2 in alkaline conditions (Fig. 12f and g). The theoretical results demonstrate that the active sites for the OER are the Fe atoms in the in situ formed NiFe oxyhydroxide. With the decoration of isolated Au atoms, the charge redistribution of the active Fe center and the adjacent atoms can lead to the promotion of the OH− adsorption process and a reduction in the adsorption energies of the OOH* and O* intermediates, thus improving the OER activity (Fig. 12h).224 In Lai's study, a series of noble metal single atoms (e.g. Ir1, Ru1, Pt1, and Pd1) were successfully anchored on an N-doped porous carbon support with dispersed Co NPs (denoted as M1@Co/NC) using a facile π-electron-assisted strategy. Interestingly, as revealed in high-magnification HAADF images (Fig. 12i), the isolated metal atoms can be stabilized on the N-doped carbon layers and Co NPs to form M1@NC and M1@Co SAAs, respectively. Among the M1@Co/NC catalysts, Ir1@Co/NC possesses the highest catalytic performance for both the OER and HER in 1.0 M KOH solution. In particular, the achieved overpotential of 260 mV at 10 mA cm−2 for the OER is significantly smaller than that of the commercial IrO2 (385 mV). Based on DFT investigations, unlike the Ir1@NC3 structure beneficial for catalyzing the HER, the active Ir1@CoO (Ir) site plays an important role in the enhancement of the OER activity. Benefiting from the unique geometric and electronic structures, the energy barrier can be effectively lowered at the Ir1@Co site, thereby achieving outstanding OER performance.228 In addition, although Rh SAs were rarely reported for the OER, Xu et al. prepared atomically dispersed Rh supported on CuO nanowire arrays on copper foam (denoted as Rh SAC–CuO NA/CF) as a highly efficient OER electrocatalyst in alkaline conditions. After atomic Rh doping by the cation exchange strategy, the Rh SAC–CuO NA/CF catalyst delivers a current density of 10 mA cm−2 at an extremely low overpotential of 197 mV, exceeding Ir/C/CF and other previously reported alkaline OER electrocatalysts. Moreover, it also shows a lower Tafel slope of 71.7 mV dec−1 compared to Ir/C/CF, Rh NP/CuO NA/CF and CuO NA/CF, demonstrating the significant role of Rh SAs in accelerating the OER kinetics. According to DFT calculations, four-coordination is the most efficient structure of Rh SAC–CuO for catalyzing the OER owing to the weaker binding ability of the O*, OH* and OOH* intermediates. Besides, to further investigate the effect of Rh SAs on the catalytic performance, different NMSAC–CuO NA/CF including Ir, Ru, Au and Os have been developed by the same method. Remarkably, Rh SAC–CuO not only has the lowest overpotential of 0.37 V, but also possesses the lowest electron transfer number of 0.97 e− between the center metal sites and the support compared to other M SAC–CuO NA/CF (M = Ir, Ru, Au, Os), revealing that Rh SAC–CuO NA/CF is highly efficient for catalyzing the OER (Fig. 12j and k). Therefore, the partial substitution of Cu atoms with single-atom Rh through a facile cation exchange reaction can greatly enhance the OER activity in alkaline conditions.229 To sum up, these studies highlight the significance of the electronic and coordination structures of atomic metal sites for optimizing the energy barrier toward the OER process, which is conducive to achieving excellent electrocatalytic OER performance.


4.2.3. Hydrogen evolution reaction (HER). 
The development of a sustainable and clean approach to generate high-purity hydrogen is highly desirable for future energy demands. Among various technologies, the electrocatalytic HER as a critical cathodic reaction in water electrolysis (2H+ + 2e− → H2) has attracted significant attention for hydrogen-related production (e.g., electrodeposition and hydrogen fuel cells) owing to the high energy conversion efficiency and environmental friendliness.28,40,230 Currently, noble-metal Pt-based catalysts are regarded as the most active electrocatalysts for the HER by virtue of the fast reaction kinetics and small overpotentials. However, their practical application in industry could be severely hampered by the low abundance and high cost.231–233 To overcome this issue, downsizing noble metal NPs or clusters to the atomic level on the support has emerged as one of the most promising strategies to maximize the atom utilization and optimize the electrocatalytic performance.234,235 In recent studies, many supported SACs based on noble metals such as Pt, Pd, Ru and Rh have been successfully prepared and employed for catalyzing the HER. For instance, Song's research group synthesized single Pt atoms anchored on onion-like carbon nanospheres (denoted as Pt1/OLC) via the ALD strategy. The OLC substrate was obtained by annealing surface-oxidized detonation nanodiamonds (DNDs) at high temperatures (1500 °C). In the HAADF-STEM images (Fig. 13a), atomically dispersed Pt can be clearly observed on the support in Pt1/OLC with the absence of Pt NPs or clusters. On the contrary, the observable aggregation of Pt atoms on the reoxidized support after heat treatment or on the support prepared at low temperatures reveals that the surface oxygen functionalities (mainly phenolic oxygen) play a role in the formation of isolated Pt atoms. According to FT-EXAFS analysis and DFT calculations, the Pt SAs are stabilized on the OLC surface by forming the stable coordination configuration of Pt–O2C1 with a high diffusion energy barrier (3.2 eV). Furthermore, the computational hydrogen evolution model suggests that the proton mass transfer to the Pt sites can be improved by the strong local electric field effect. Therefore, the optimized Pt1/OLC electrocatalyst exhibits superior HER performance (η10 = −38 mV and TOF = 40.78 H2 s−1 at 100 mV) comparable to commercial Pt/C, and exceptional stability in acidic media.236 In another study, a nitrogen dopant was introduced into the carbon support to anchor noble metal single atoms. Owing to the strong metal–support interaction, singly dispersed Pt atoms can be stabilized on hierarchical nitrogen-doped carbon nanocages (Pt1/hNCNC) with high Pt loading (2.92 wt%). In HER tests, Pt1/hNCNC-2.92 demonstrates a η10 of 15 mV and a Tafel slope of 24 mV dec−1 in acidic conditions, outperforming Pt/hCNC without N-doping. Specifically, this catalyst also gives a mass activity 18.5 times higher (7.60 A mgPt−1) than that of commercial Pt/C, as well as extraordinary stability (for 10000 cycles) (Fig. 13b). From the XPS spectra (Fig. 13c), Pt1/hNCNC shows a lower binding energy in comparison to Pt1/hCNC, revealing the higher electron density of isolated Pt atoms in the Pt1/hNCNC catalyst as a result of the N dopant anchoring effects. DFT studies further indicate that the N participation can effectively improve the ability to capture [PtCl6]2− anions and stabilize the derived single Pt atoms by electrostatic interactions combined with micropore trapping, which offers a facile and convenient route for constructing various NMSACs (Fig. 13d).237 Besides the carbon support, other substrates such as metal oxides and metal sulfides have also been applied for the synthesis of highly efficient NMSACs. Zhang and co-workers prepared double transition metal MXene nanosheets to immobilize single Pt atoms by an electrochemical exfoliation strategy (denoted as Mo2TiC2Tx–PtSA). Compared with pristine Mo2TiC2Tx, electrochemically exfoliated Mo2TiC2Tx-VMo and commercial Pt/C, the as-prepared Mo2TiC2Tx–PtSA shows a superior HER performance with an overpotential of 30, 77 and 104 mV at a current density of 10, 100 and 200 mA cm−2, respectively, as well as a small Tafel slope of 30 mV dec−1 in 0.5 M H2SO4 solution (Fig. 13e and f). Moreover, the Mo2TiC2Tx–PtSA catalyst can also possess comparable catalytic activity to commercial Pt/C in neutral media. Results from FT-EXAFS analysis demonstrate that the atomic Pt sites in the rich Mo vacancies are coordinated with three C atoms and two O-terminal atoms to form Pt–C and Pt–O bonds. Further XANES measurements confirm the positively charged single Pt atoms in the catalyst, which can be attributed to the electron transfer from Pt atoms to the support. Based on DFT calculations, the incorporation of isolated Pt atoms into the MXene surface can not only lead to higher electronic energy levels, but also promote electron transfer. Benefiting from the strong covalent bonding between the positively charged individual Pt atoms and MXene support, the developed Mo2TiC2Tx–PtSA demonstrates enhanced catalytic performance and excellent stability for the HER.238
 Fig. 13  (a) HAADF-STEM image of Pt1/OLC. Reproduced with permission.236 Copyright 2019, Nature Publishing Group. (b) HER durability test of Pt1/hNCNC, Pt/hCNC and commercial Pt/C in 0.5 M H2SO4 solution. (c) XPS Pt 4f spectra of Pt1/hNCNC and Pt/hCNC. (d) Six typical configurations of [PtCl6]2− on various supports and their corresponding calculated free energies. Reproduced with permission.237 Copyright 2019, Nature Publishing Group. (e) HER polarization curves of Mo2TiC2Tx–PtSA, Mo2TiC2Tx–VMo, Mo2TiC2Tx, CP and Pt/C in 0.5 M H2SO4 solution. (f) Corresponding Tafel slopes derived from (e). Reproduced with permission.238 Copyright 2018, Nature Publishing Group. (g) The Ru K-edge k2-weighted EXAFS spectra of Ni5P4–Ru, RuO2 and Ru foil. (h) The optimized structure for H2O adsorption on different sites. (i) The energy barriers for water dissociation on Ni5P4 and Ni5P4–Rusurf. Reproduced with permission.88 Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH. 
Recently, the development of Ru-based SACs has become the major focus for the HER due to the relatively cheap price and similar metal–hydrogen bond strength in comparison to Pt. For instance, He and co-workers reported individual Ru atoms supported on the surface of Ni5P4 (Ni5P4–Ru) through an impregnation–phosphorization strategy. Based on inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) analysis, the mass ratio of Ru/Ni in Ni(OH)2–Ru with rich nickel vacancies (V-Ni(OH)2–Ru) is higher than that of Ni(OH)2–Ru with fewer nickel vacancies (P-Ni(OH)2–Ru), demonstrating the essential role of the interaction between Ru3+ and nickel-vacancy defects in achieving high-density single-site Ru incorporation, which can be also confirmed in electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) results. After phosphorization, the peaks corresponding to Ru–Ni and Ru–P bonds with the absence of Ru–Ru bonds in the EXAFS spectra (Fig. 13g) reveal the existence of single Ru atoms in Ni5P4. As expected, the developed Ni5P4–Ru loaded on carbon cloth (Ni5P4–Ru/CC) possesses comparable catalytic activity to the commercial Pt/C catalyst toward the HER in alkaline conditions, with an overpotential of 54 mV to achieve 10 mA cm−2 and a small Tafel slope of 52 mV dec−1. To investigate the role of atomic Ru in the catalyst, DFT calculations were conducted to evaluate the structure and catalytic properties of Ni5P4–Ru and pristine Ni5P4 (Fig. 13h and i). Owing to the localized structural polarization, the electron density increase of single-atom Ru in Ni5P4–Ru can effectively reduce the energy barrier for water dissociation at Ru sites (1.28 eV). In addition, the lower hydrogen adsorption free energy (ΔGH*) of −0.30 eV in comparison to pristine Ni5P4 (−0.42 eV at the P site) also verifies the positive effect of the incorporated Ru sites in enhancing the HER activity.88 Besides, other noble metal-based SACs such as Pd, Rh and Ir also exhibit their application potential for catalyzing the HER. In Yu's study, atomically dispersed Pd was successfully anchored on ultrathin graphdiyne nanosheets (GDY) with a Pd loading of 0.2 wt%. Compared with the commercial Pt/C, Pd0/GDY demonstrates a superior mass activity of 61.5 A mgmetal−1 and TOF of 16.7 s−1 in acidic conditions, which can be ascribed to the well-defined structures and active zero-valent Pd atoms. Moreover, benefiting from the strong interaction between the isolated Pd atoms and GDY support as well as the inert nature of GDY, Pd0/GDY also possesses excellent stability during long-term operation.239 In addition, Meng and co-workers developed a single-atom Rh catalyst confined in MoS2, achieving a low overpotential of 67 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2. Evidenced from DFT calculations, a distance synergy between the isolated Rh atoms confined in the MoS2 lattice can not only play an essential role in adjusting the electronic structure of the adjacent S atoms, but also in optimizing the hydrogen adsorption energy on the S sites, thereby boosting the HER performance.240 The above examples provide new insights into rational design of various high-performance NMSACs for electrochemical hydrogen production.


4.2.4. CO2 reduction reaction (CO2RR). 
The excessive consumption of fossil fuels has induced increasing greenhouse CO2 emission and consequent environmental concerns. In this regard, room-temperature electrochemical reduction conversion of CO2 into valuable products driven by renewable electricity is considered one of the most promising approaches to reduce CO2 emission and remit the energy crisis.241–243 Nevertheless, the CO2RR process is usually hindered by its high overpotential, undesirable conversion efficiency and poor selectivity, which can be ascribed to the chemically inert properties of CO2 and the presence of the competitive HER process in the electrolyte. Therefore, tremendous efforts have been devoted to preparing highly active electrocatalysts for promoting the sluggish kinetics of the CO2RR and achieving high selectivity toward specific products such as CO, CH3OH, CH4 and HCOOH.106,242,244,245 To date, many SACs featuring maximum atom utilization efficiency and low coordination environments have been synthesized and applied for catalyzing the CO2RR.40 Among them, precious metal-based SACs including Pd, Au and Ag have been shown to be favorable to produce CO from electrocatalytic CO2 reduction.246
Combining the advantages of the weakly binding Au sites and strongly binding Pd sites, Wang et al. prepared a series of Pd ensembles on Au surfaces with different Pd dispersions (denoted as Pd@Au) to investigate the ensemble effects of Pd sites for electroreduction of CO2-to-CO. From the HAADF-STEM and EELS results (Fig. 14a–e), dispersed discrete points associated with the single Pd sites on the Au surface can be clearly observed when the atomic ratio of Pd is 2% and 5% (Pd2@Au98 and Pd5@Au95). As the atomic Pd ratio increases to 10% and 20%, a semicontinuous Pd layer and Pd shell are formed in Pd10@Au90 and Pd20@Au80, respectively, due to the growth of Pd ensembles on the surface. In addition, results from EXAFS (Fig. 14f–i) reveal that the total coordination number (CN) of Au is estimated to be around 8 including a CNAu–Pd of ∼1 and CNAu–Au of ∼7 for all the Pd@Au, further demonstrating that most Pd atoms are anchored on the Au surface. At a 0.5 V potential, Pd5@Au95 achieves a partial current density (JCO) of 1.6 mA cmgeo−2 and faradaic efficiency (FECO) of ∼80%, outperforming other Pd@Au catalysts. Specifically, the corresponding mass activity of 178 A gmetal−1 is higher than that of pure Au NPs and most of the electrocatalysts for the CO2RR. Moreover, Pd5@Au95 also exhibits excellent stability during continuous 24 h operation with only a small degradation, superior to pure Pd. As confirmed by DFT calculations, the enhanced electrocatalytic CO2RR activity and stability of Pd5@Au95 can be ascribed to the lower energy barrier for CO2 activation in comparison to pure Au, and the reduced sensitivity to CO poisoning by forming a stronger combination with *CO intermediates compared to pure Pd at the atomically dispersed Pd ensembles. Owing to the effect of the Pd ensemble size on the adsorption energies of the *CO and *COOH intermediates, the CO2RR activity of Pd@Au toward CO production shows a nonlinear behavior dependent on the surface Pd composition.241 More recently, He and co-workers reported a single-atom Pd catalyst supported on N-doped carbon (Pd–NC), which can reach a higher FECO of 55% at −0.5 V vs. RHE and JCO of 0.244 mA cm−2 at −0.4 V vs. RHE than those of Pd/C for electrocatalytic CO2-to-CO (Fig. 14j and k). Both XAFS and HAADF-STEM studies suggest that the Pd–N4 structure can be formed in Pd–NC by the coordination of the isolated Pd atom with four N atoms. Further in situ XAFS analysis was conducted to investigate the local structure variation of the Pd sites in the Pd–NC and Pd/C catalysts. Compared with Pd/C, the absence of peak position shifts for Pd–NC in both XANES and EXAFS reveals the stable Pd–N4 configuration without palladium hydride (PdH) formation in the electroreduction of CO2 to CO. Moreover, based on DFT calculations (Fig. 14l and m), the significant difference between the binding energies of *H and *HOCO (2.2 eV) and weak binding energy for *CO desorption (−0.02 eV) at Pd–N4 sites confirm the important role of the Pd–N4 moiety in enhancing the CO2RR performance toward CO in Pd–NC. Therefore, this work offers a facile and promising strategy to prepare cost-effective and highly efficient CO2RR electrocatalysts.246 On the basis of the above studies, more attention should be focused on optimizing the coordination structures of NMSACs for electrocatalytic CO2 reduction. In addition, more experimental research and theoretical calculations are also required due to the complex reaction mechanism and diverse reaction products.
 Fig. 14  (a) Schematic illustration of the synthetic process for Pd@Au NPs with precise control over the dose of Pd. HAADF-STEM images and EELS results for (b) Pd2@Au98, (c) Pd5@Au95, (d) Pd10@Au90 and (e) Pd2@Au80. k2-Weighted FT spectra at the (f) Au L-edge and (g) Pd K-edge. The coordination numbers from the EXAFS analysis for (h) Au atoms and (i) Pd atoms. Reproduced with permission.241 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society. (j) Faradaic efficiencies (FE) for CO evolution at different applied potentials. (k) Partial current densities of CO evolution at different applied potentials. (l) The optimized structures of *H, *CO and *HOCO adsorbed on Pd–N4 embedded on a 4 × 4 graphene supercell based on DFT calculations. (m) The binding energy difference between H and HOCO on Pd–N4, PdH(111) and Pd(111). Reproduced with permission.246 Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH. 


4.2.5. Nitrogen reduction reaction (NRR). 
As an important nitrogen source for agricultural fertilizer and a desirable clean energy carrier, ammonia (NH3) is commonly synthesized through the energy-consuming and unsustainable Haber–Bosch process (HBP) (N2 + 3H2 ⇌ 2NH3) at high pressures and temperatures in industrial production.247,248 To achieve efficient and green nitrogen fixation, electrocatalytic reduction of earth abundant N2 to NH3 (N2 + 6H+ + 6e− → 2NH3) has become an expanding field and is regarded as an alternative to the traditional HBP owing to the mild operational conditions and utilization of renewable energy. However, the high energy requirement for the breakage of NN triple bonds (941 kJ mol−1) and the competition with the HER in aqueous solution can lead to several issues such as high overpotentials, low faradaic efficiency (FENH3) and poor NH3 yield. Hence, numerous efforts have been dedicated to exploring highly active electrocatalysts to lower the energy barriers and improve the efficiency toward the NRR.1,26,249
So far, many NMSACs such as Ru, Au and Pt have been developed and exhibited great potential for reducing N2 into NH3 in electrocatalytic reactions.250 In the work reported by Zeng's group, monodisperse Ru atoms stabilized on N-doped carbon (named Ru SA/N–C) were developed by pyrolysis of Ru-containing ZIF-8 precursors. As confirmed by the EXAFS measurement, a major peak located at around 2.04 Å denotes the formation of Ru–N bonds. The absence of peaks for metallic Ru or its oxidized clusters further reveals the presence of atomic Ru throughout the whole structure, which is consistent with the X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) (Fig. 15a) and HAADF-STEM results. In 0.05 M H2SO4 solution, the prepared Ru SA/N–C demonstrates a superior FENH3 of 29.6% at −0.2 V vs. RHE, which is twice as high as that of Ru NP/N–C. Specifically, an extremely high NH3 yield rate of 120.9 μgNH3 mgcat.−1 h−1 can be achieved at −0.2 V (vs. RHE), outperforming the reported value for the NRR. Moreover, according to both the experimental measurements conducted by N2 temperature-programmed desorption (N2-TPD) and theoretical calculations (Fig. 15b and c), the exceptional NRR performance of Ru SA/N–C toward NH3 production can be ascribed to the strong binding energy of N2 and reduced Gibbs free-energy variation (ΔG) for the N2 dissociation step on single-site Ru, thereby resulting in superior activity in comparison to Ru NP/N–C.62 In another case, Tao and co-workers synthesized a ZrO2/NC-supported Ru SAC (0.1 wt% Ru loading) via a simple coordination-assisted strategy, which achieves an excellent NH3 yield rate of 3.665 mgNH3 h−1 mgRu−1 at −0.21 V vs. RHE and a high FENH3 of ∼21% at a low overpotential of 0.17 V. Based on XPS measurements, the lower value of the Zr 3d5/2 peak (182.1 eV) in comparison to that of ZrO2 (182.8 eV) reveals that O vacancies can be formed on the exposed ZrO2 surface after pyrolysis under an N2 atmosphere. Further DFT calculations demonstrate that atomic Ru can be effectively anchored on the O-vacancy sites in ZrO2 as the main active centers to improve *NNH stabilization, *H destabilization and N2 adsorption, thereby achieving high selectivity toward the NRR/HER. Moreover, combined with the XPS results, N doping plays a role in preventing the aggregation of single Ru atoms and improving their stabilization on the surface. Thus, the enhanced electroreduction of N2 to NH3 can be mainly attributed to the suppression effect of Ru@ZrO2 on the HER and promotion effect of Ru@NC on the NRR.247 Except for MOF-derived carbon supports with porous structure and high surface area, other materials such as MXene nanosheets with metal atoms (e.g., Mo) have also been employed to anchor isolated atoms for promoting the electrocatalytic NRR activity under ambient conditions. In Peng's study, the as-prepared single-atom Ru catalyst supported on Mo2CTx nanosheets (SA Ru–Mo2CTx) with an Ru loading of 1.41 wt% delivers a remarkable NH3 yield rate of 40.57 μg h−1 mg−1 as well as a high FE of 25.77% at −0.3 V vs. RHE for NH3 generation, superior to the commercial Ru/C, bare Mo2CTx and most reported NRR catalysts in 0.5 M K2SO4 electrolyte. As revealed in DFT calculations (Fig. 15d), the successful stabilization of atomic Ru sites on the Mo2CTx nanosheets as electron back-donation centers exhibits a positive effect on achieving enhanced N2 adsorption and activation, lowering the energy barrier for hydrogenation of *N2 to *NNH. Therefore, the development of 2D MXene-based catalysts combined with the atomic-doping engineering strategy offers a promising direction for promoting electrocatalytic N2 fixation.251
 Fig. 15  (a) XRD patterns of Ru SA/N–C and Ru NP/N–C. (b) The N2-TPD profiles of Ru SA/N–C and Ru NP/N–C. (c) Free energy diagram of the NRR with a distal pathway on Ru1–N3, Ru1–N4, and Ru(101). Reproduced with permission.62 Copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH. (d) The charge density difference calculations of N2 bonded to Ru and Mo atoms in SA Ru–Mo2CTx. Reproduced with permission.251 Copyright 2020, Wiley-VCH. (e) FE and yield rate of NH3 at different applied potentials. Reproduced with permission.252 Copyright 2018, Wiley-VCH. (f) End-on configurations of *N2 atoms and *NNH admolecules on SA-Ag/NC. (g) Ag K-edge XANES spectra and (h) FT-EXAFS curves of SA-Ag/NC, Ag2O and Ag foil. (i) Chronoamperometry result of the reduction current density for SA-Ag/NC at −0.60 V vs. RHE. Reproduced with permission.253 Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
In addition to Ru-based SACs, Au- and Ag-based SACs have also been investigated as highly active and stable NRR electrocatalysts. For example, atomically dispersed Au anchored on N-doped porous carbon (denoted as AuSA–NDPC) was prepared by Qin and co-workers using a facile impregnation method. Owing to the abundant N species on the support surface, isolated Au atoms (Lewis acid ions) can be trapped and coordinated with electron-rich N atoms (Lewis basic sites) to form a stable structure (a frustrated Lewis pair), which acts as active sites for promoting N2 dissociation. Moreover, the highly polarizable porous structure formed by the condensation of hexaazatriphenylene–hexacarbonitrile (HAT) and subsequent silica removal is beneficial for exposing more active sites and facilitating mass transfer. Benefiting from these outstanding characteristics, the as-prepared AuSA–NDPC shows a high yield rate of 2.32 μgNH3 h−1 cm−2 with a desirable FE of 12.3% for NH3 production at −0.2 V (vs. RHE) (Fig. 15e).252 In addition, an N-doped carbon black-supported single-site Ag catalyst (SA-Ag/NC) was reported to be highly efficient for catalyzing electroreduction of N2 into NH3. According to DFT calculations (Fig. 15f), both the vertical end-on configuration of N2 adsorption and oblique end-on configuration of *NNH admolecule formation on atomic metal sites are favorable for achieving high electrocatalytic NRR performance. Inspired by this, Ag–N4 coordination is selected as a suitable candidate to target the two admolecule configurations. Due to the successful formation of Ag–N4 confirmed by XANES and EXAFS measurements (Fig. 15g and h), SA-Ag/NC displays excellent NRR activity with an NH3 faradaic efficiency of 21.9% at −0.60 V vs. RHE and a stable yield of 270.9 μg h−1 mgcat.−1 at −0.65 V vs. RHE in 0.1 M HCl electrolyte. Furthermore, both the yield rate and FE value can be maintained almost unchanged in 20 consecutive reaction cycles as well as a constant reduction current density for 60 h, confirming the long-term durability of this catalyst (Fig. 15i).253 All these recent developments in the NRR provide the possibility of NMSACs as promising candidates to achieve sustainable and efficient NH3 synthesis.


4.2.6. Electrooxidation of methanol/formic acid. 
With the growing energy demands, serious environmental pollution issues and rising traditional fossil fuel consumption, the development of cost-effective and highly efficient energy storage and conversion technologies is highly desirable for solving the existing problems. Nowadays, fuel cells have been emerging as one of the promising alternatives for achieving a sustainable energy economy owing to their high energy conversion efficiency and environmentally friendly merits.26,80,254 Among them, liquid fuels such as methanol and formic acid derived from biomass have aroused great interest for fuel cell applications as important chemical feedstocks. In particular, the feedstock transportation for direct methanol fuel cells (DMFCs) and direct formic acid fuel cells (DFAFCs) is much easier and safer in comparison to the PEMFC system fed by H2. Currently, high-performance precious metal Pt-based electrocatalysts have been commonly applied to accelerate the sluggish kinetics of key steps: the methanol oxidation reaction (MOR, CH3OH + H2O → 6H+ + 6e− + CO2) in DMFCs and the formic acid oxidation reaction (FAOR, HCOOH → CO2 + 2H+ + 2e−) in DFAFCs. However, their widespread commercialization is still severely inhibited by the economic barriers and low tolerance to the reaction intermediates (e.g., CO). Hence, it is expected to explore facile and effective strategies to maximize the Pt atom utilization efficiency and enhance the catalytic activity and stability in selective methanol/formic acid electrooxidation.1,26,80
Methanol oxidation reaction. 
The MOR process occurring at the anode in DMFCs consists of ‘direct’ and ‘indirect’ pathways to produce the CO2 product. In the direct pathway, the soluble intermediates (e.g., formic acid and formaldehyde) can be formed firstly and then oxidized to CO2, which requires 1 to 2 Pt atoms. In contrast, the indirect pathway involves CO formation and requires more Pt atoms (3–4) in the process. Since the latter route is the predominant one for the MOR, Pt ensembles are more conducive to facilitating CO intermediate adsorption. However, some previous studies reported that reducing the Pt particle size to clusters or single atoms is cost-effective and efficient for improving the catalytic activity and CO tolerance.80,255 For instance, Sun and co-workers found that isolated Pt atoms deposited on graphene nanosheets via the ALD strategy (50 ALD cycles, denoted as 50ALDPt/GNS) could act as an outstanding electrocatalyst for the MOR. To investigate the Pt particle size effect on the performance, several Pt/graphene samples with different particle sizes from single atoms to nanoparticles were synthesized by controlling the number of ALD cycles. Obviously, both the TEM images and inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES) test reveal that the average size of Pt particles and Pt loading increase with increasing ALD cycles. As seen in the further HAADF-STEM images (Fig. 16a–c), the Pt particles mainly exist as the single atom form when the ALD cycle number is 50, while subnanometer Pt clusters and small Pt nanoparticles can also be observed in 50ALDPt/GNS. After the number of ALD cycles is increased to 100 or even 150, relatively larger Pt clusters and particles are formed owing to the agglomeration and growth of single atoms in the catalysts. For electrooxidation of methanol, 50ALDPt/GNS shows the lowest overpotential of 0.79 V (vs. RHE) and the highest current density of 22.9 mA cm−2, outperforming those of 100ALDPt/GNS (0.82 V vs. RHE, 8.48 mA cm−2) and 150ALDPt/GNS (0.85 V vs. RHE, 5.73 mA cm−2) (Fig. 16d). Therefore, enhanced catalytic activity can be achieved at a reduced Pt particle size. Significantly, 50ALDPt/GNS possesses a superior electrocatalytic MOR performance and better CO tolerance in comparison to commercial Pt/C, which can be ascribed to the more unsaturated 5d orbitals of single-atom Pt and stronger interaction between Pt atoms and graphene confirmed in XAFS and DFT calculations.254
 Fig. 16  The magnified HAADF-STEM images of Pt/GNS samples with (a) 50 ALD cycles, (b) 100 ALD cycles and (c) 150 ALD cycles. (d) CVs of methanol oxidation on ALD50Pt/GNS, ALD100Pt/GNS, ALD150Pt/GNS and Pt/C. Reproduced with permission.254 Copyright 2013, Nature Publishing Group. (e) Pt mass-normalized anodic sweeps obtained from PtAu NPs in a mixed solution containing 0.1 M concentrations of both HClO4 and HCOOH. (f) Schematic illustration of the CO adsorption modes on model (111) lattices of pure Pt surfaces, few-atom Pt surfaces and single-atom Pt surfaces as well as calculated adsorption energies for the CO adsorption sites. Reproduced with permission.257 Copyright 2018, Nature Publishing Group. (g) The mass activity change at 0.6 V of Pt1/ATO, Pt NP/ATO and Pt/C during the durability test with 1800 repeated CV cycles in the range of 0.05–1.4 V. (h) FAOR forward scans after the durability test in Ar-saturated 0.1 M HClO4 with 0.5 M HCOOH solutions. Reproduced with permission.258 Copyright 2017, Wiley-VCH. (i) Peak mass activity of SA-Rh/CN, Pd/C, Pt/C and Rh/C. Reproduced with permission.63 Copyright 2020, Nature Publishing Group. 
Formic acid oxidation reaction. 
Electrooxidation of formic acid is a significant reaction in DFAFCs, which demonstrates less toxicity, higher kinetic activity and an easier process compared to the MOR in DMFCs.1,256 Similarly, the FAOR can also proceed via two different routes, involving the direct pathway (HCOOH → CO2 + 2H+ + 2e−) and indirect pathway (HCOOH → H2O + COads). However, unlike the MOR, the direct pathway plays a dominant role in the FAOR process. In this case, the design and fabrication of Pt SACs are highly desirable for providing efficient reactive sites and achieving high utilization efficiency of Pt atoms.30 In 2018, Duchesne et al. developed a series of bimetallic PtAu NPs with Pt loadings ranging from 4% to 96% by adopting a facile colloidal synthetic method. Based on cyclic voltammetry analysis (Fig. 16e), the Pt4Au96 catalyst demonstrates a superior activity at the first Pt-mass-normalized current peaks (∼0.6 VRHE), which is 126 and 90 times as high as that of the least-active Pt78Au22 and commercial Pt/C, respectively. In addition, the absence of the second peak attributed to the electrooxidation of adsorbed CO molecules at approximately 0.85 VRHE implies the important role of resistance to CO poisoning in improving the electrocatalytic FAOR activity. As confirmed in further DFT calculations (Fig. 16f), the generated atomically dispersed Pt sites on the surface and electronic effects originating from Pt–Au bonding interactions can lead to weakened CO adsorption at both few-atom and single-atom Pt surfaces, revealing that the single Pt adsorption sites are responsible for the dehydrogenation of formic acid, thereby achieving exceptional FAOR activity.257 In addition, a single-atom Pt catalyst anchored on antimony-doped tin oxide (denoted as Pt1/ATO) with a Pt loading up to 8 wt% was prepared by Kim and co-workers using an incipient wetness impregnation method. The XRD results accompanied by DFT calculations indicate that the introduced Pt atoms can replace Sb sites in the bulk phase or at the ATO or SbSn surface to form an energetically stable structure. In the electrocatalytic FAOR performance test, Pt1/ATO possesses a higher mass activity than that of Pt NP/ATO and Pt/C at 0.6 V, demonstrating the exceptional FAOR activity. Moreover, the maintained high activity and relatively small loss of the Pt amount after the durability test with 1800 repeating cycles further confirm the excellent stability of Pt1/ATO under harsh conditions (Fig. 16g and h). Specifically, the effects of different Pt content in Pt1/ATO were also investigated. At around 0.6 V, 1 wt% Pt1/ATO shows a superior mass activity of 9.16 A mgPt−1 in comparison to that of 8 wt% Pt1/ATO (0.89 A mg−1), which can be explained by the fact that most of the single Pt atoms are located inside the ATO support in 8 wt% Pt1/ATO, instead of on the surface.258 Besides, other NMSACs such as Rh and Ir have also been reported as economical and efficient alternatives to commercial Pt/C. Recently, Xiong and co-workers demonstrated that single Rh atoms supported on ZIF-8-derived N-doped carbon (denoted as SA-Rh/CN) could show outstanding catalytic activity with a remarkable mass activity of 16.1 A mg−1 at the peak potential and a high TOF value of 8.55 s−1, exceeding those of the state-of-the-art Pt/C and Pd/C (Fig. 16i). Due to the relatively high activation barrier to generate CO at isolated Rh sites as well as unfavorable combination with CO, the tolerance to CO poisoning in SA-Rh/CN can be improved, thereby realizing superior catalytic stability.63 Similarly, various NMSACs (e.g., Pt, Ir, Pd and Ru) supported on N-doped carbon were also synthesized by the host–guest strategy in Li's study. Among them, Ir/CN demonstrates the highest catalytic activity, which can achieve the highest current density of 12.9 A mgIr−1 at 0.7 V and excellent CO resistance. Therefore, this work encourages the development of new single-atom electrocatalysts for an efficient FAOR.259
All the fabrication approaches for SACs aim to prevent the aggregation of isolated metal atoms into NPs/nanoclusters and strengthen the metal–support interactions. Based on the above-mentioned examples, the development of novel and efficient synthetic strategies is beneficial for preparing ideal NMSACs, which hold great promise for enhancing the catalytic activity and selectivity in selective methanol/formic acid electrooxidation reactions.

4.3. Photocatalysis
Nowadays, photocatalysis has been recognized as a sustainable and promising approach for chemical and energy production, which is designed to facilitate solar-to-chemical transformation with the participation of catalysts.4,316,317 Many efforts have been devoted to fabricating efficient photocatalysts for various photocatalytic reactions such as photocatalytic water splitting and CO2 reduction reactions, while the low visible light utilization rate and quantum yield, and poor stability are still the main hindrances for their practical applications. Inspired by the 100% utilization efficiency of metals, robust intrinsic activity and excellent stability during catalytic processes, NMSACs have become an active research field in recent studies.25,30,318 In the following section, the development of NMSACs as high-performance photocatalysts is introduced in several photocatalytic reactions, especially photocatalytic H2 evolution. The performances of these catalysts are also summarized in Table 4.
